GAME: Goa
RANK: 101
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Elegant and simple mechanics, probably one of the best implementation of an auction system, great variety of options and paths to victory, fast to play and easy to learn. It's good with any number of players, you learn its main dynamics just after the first turn and then it's all a matter of choices, trying to balance your long term strategy to the opportunities that every turn presents. You have to maximize the effectiveness of every action you have at your disposal, while managing your resources in order to gain victory points and more actions. The system is simple enough to give you always the right perspective on your situation and let you concentrate on your options, to the point that, at the end of the game, you can easily spot the errors in your strategy, what went wrong and how it did. There’s not much direct interaction among the players and theme is not exactly dripping from the mechanics, but it’s one of those rare cases in which I don’t mind.

Great (as usual) Hans im Glück quality: good components, well designed and functional. You could use a playing aid about exploration cards and tile use, but a couple of plays is all you need to learn the meaning of every symbol.

P.S. remember to use the right rule about the exploration path: first discard the cards that would be in excess and then draw the new ones.

RATING: 10
Individual action/optimization strategy game with an auction phase central to each round.

You're a spice trader who has VERY limited actions to get spice to ship, ships to ship the spices, money to purchase new and helpful items, action cards that allow you special benefits, and risky ventures to find new and valuable spice colonies.

Each turn you have to decide if you're going to advance (which you need spice and ships to do), or if you're going to "cash in" at the particular level you're at (which you often need to do).  The further you advance, the more points you get, and the greater the benefits when you "cash in".  You'll always want money, because it's crucial to purchase items in the auction phase, plus most money is worth points at the end of the game.  You won't be able to resist grabbing action cards, even though their benefits encompass a very wide range, and they're also worth points at the end of the game.  And who can resist trying to found new spice colonies, since they not only give you points, but also valuable spice storage capabilites.  You're also tempted to move down the columns evenly, in order to moderately increase points across the board, and to obtain the coveted extra actions, but there is also the desire to go deep in a column to further increase points and obtain free action cards.

The auction phase is central to the game, has some innovation to it (re: the flag-bearer), and it actually works exceptionally well from 2-4 players.  In fact, the overall game plays equally well with 2-4 players, and ranks among the more strategic and most requested games by myself, my wife, and my friends.

RATING: 7
1 play / 3 players

LIKE: A classic, dry game that has held up because of its unique spacial-auction mechanism and tightly woven upgrade system. I enjoyed the feeling that everything felt balanced and I wanted one-more action. I also enjoyed how the money could shift to another player during the auctions, as this added a nice extra option when decided on tiles to auction.

DISLIKE: A money-snowball effect can happen if tiles are not valued and bid upon properly in the opening game. The game is dry, and modern gamers are less enthusiastic about it.

OVERALL: I like it. I wish it would get an update and available to more people. I feel that from a high-level this game doesn't stand out as much as it should, other than the central auction. Art or theme change would address this. Considering how long ago this game came out, I wish other designers used it as a bar for their designs, as everything is so tightly packaged together.

(Side note: The game is almost language independent once the iconography is deciphered.)

RATING: 10
The best new game I have played in 2004. The blending of resources is sublime. 

The immediate view of its quality was to compare this game with Puerto Rico and Princes of Florence, both of which it resembles in various ways. 

While there is some solitaire planning, you do need to follow what others are doing and the auctions of course do involve all players. 

Even after playing this a few times, there appear to be multiple routes to victory, which suggests the game is as promising as it feels.

There is no doubt in my mind that this is a 10 game for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
If only we could play more often!  It's a great game, but it is always [i]stressful[/i] -- every move counts.  So it doesn't work as an evening's fun, and leaves you thinking long after the game is over.  The tiles (yes the tiles, not the expedition cards) serve the purpose of cards in Agricola, varying the game with each play -- but Dorn's done it better, in that in Agricola most cards are fairly weak, while in Goa the tiles can be critical -- but you get to bid on them, which makes it less luck-dependent.

True, the expedition cards can introduce random luck, when both players play well, but a little luck isn't bad.  Not every game must be Caylus.

What's more, as our plays increase, it becomes less stressful, and the rules are surprisingly easy to remember even if you go a month or two without playing.  It's just a great game!

RATING: 5

I have since downloaded a bunch of game and player aids to help us in case we do get a game in (and I can already tell they'll help big time). Since Goa is so highly rated as a 2-player game, I want to play again before I rate it.

UPDATE 1: Finally got a chance to play Goa with 4 players, and it took all of us nearly forever to figure out the rules based on the rule book. However, well worth the effort! For me, Goa is in the same category of games as Puerto Rico, Caylus, and Power Grid - except Goa is way better! I will elaborate another time! UPDATE 2: Lowered my rating due to painful headaches that appear when I think about the rule book - 2 hours to go over the rules and find answers to basic questions!

RATING: 8
2-4(4)

New version. It does feel a bit dated in the components and how spare the game mechanisms are (games seem to be gravitating toward "kitchen sink" designs). But, nevertheless, it has a solid auction system that holds up for two players and then just a lot of constraint on your choices and what you'll be able to accomplish within the game. Upgrades on the various tracks are essential, as you hate to take actions when you've not upgraded, but you need to balance that with actually doing things.

A fine Euro that holds up (better than Princes of Florence, for example).

RATING: 8
A very solid Euro with a bit of everything, from bidding to action selection and combo making (yes, that's not a game mechanism, but there's no other way to describe it). 

Good things: 
- it's interesting all the way until the end
- there are plenty of options 
- the luck element does not make or break the game or the winner

Bad things:
- one strategic mistake and you're out of the path to victory. The most you can still hope for is that every other player will make at least 1 mistake.

Neutral:
- people with good memory will have an advantage, I was able to keep track of everyone's money until the end of game, which gave me a strategic advantage at auctions and 3 VP for richest player

RATING: 9
3/20/08 Played at Matt and Brad's place with Sterling, Joshua and Brad. Sterling and Joshua had played the game before. Basically you have money and and bid each round on plantations (that give can produce goods), various special abilities and special actions. Before the game even starts you need to decide upon which strategy you will implement in the game. Although through the bidding process you strategy might be foiled. That is unfortunate but you must still maintain a clear idea/strategy. This game rewards planning and implementation. It is not very forgiving. I don't think it would play well with less than 4 players. Overall Goa is a very complex Auction/Bidding euro game. After 1 play I would rate it a 8.

RATING: 6
Possible MPS.

4-28-2007
First game.  This game was definitely inoffensive yet not exciting.  This game just doesn't excite me all that much.  Don't get me wrong.  I like economic games, but this just didn't give me a very good first impression.  The mechanics are sound enough and interesting enough.  The flag is a pretty cool mechanic and how bidding revolves around it.  The biggest strike for me against this game is the one time bidding.  I just don't like that very much.  I don't know, maybe then if we did it power grid style, then I would complain about the game being too long.  Another thing that bugs me about this game is that it seems that there is one set strategy, which is to upgrade your Expedition Track all the time so you can get those crazy widly varied powerful expedition cards.  You then build your short term strategy as to take advantage of the expedition cards that you recieved.  Rinse and repeat.  I also do not like how convoluted the scoring is.  I guess it is good to recieve points for all the various things that you do but they are not linear so it gets tedious scoring at the end of the game.  I guess we will see how my impression of the game becomes.  For now this falls in mediocre category for me.

RATING: 7.5
Goa is an unusual auction game, with an auction mechanic that allows a challenging and engaging play even with only 2 players. It is also a resource management game, with a race like feeling, because players receive rewards to reach certain goals before the others.
Every element in the game is so interwoven with the others that the game can be very hard to explain, and quite heavy to play, so I don't know how much play it will see... (but the not-so-long playtime can give it an edge!).
Nevertheless, it is a wonderful game, and it starts with a 7.5. Hopefully I will play it some more times to raise its rating...

RATING: 8
This is a wonderful game that combines an auction/bidding stage with an action point stage, similar to Princes of Florence.  The players always bid on the starting flag, but then each get to pick one item to auction afterwards, making the placement of auction pieces key.  After the auction round is complete each player gets a minimum of three actions, though extra actions are available, and valuable, through a couple different methods.  Even if you have an extra action each turn, you still want more.  There is a really nice balance between resources, economics, auction and action and is a highly recommended game.

RATING: 7
An auction based game with what appears to be several strategies to acquire points and win. You manage Dutch colonies in the East Indies and are trying to improve various parts of this endeavor, such as farms, shipping, colonists, etc. I enjoyed the theme and competing with other players to acquire the stuff I needed or make them pay dearly for allowing them to get it. The once around auction style makes for quick turns, necessary because there are 5 auctions every turn in a four player game. 

I won the fewest auctions in my only game, always concerned about not paying too much for things, even though I wasn't sure what that was. Because I kept losing auctions even when bidding what I though was high, I concentrated on colonies and won the match!

RATING: 7.5
Took three games to get the rules right but this is now a top 10 favourite. The mechanisms are beautifully balanced with the constant feeling that you need just one more action to do what you need. Sharp focus on what is achievable needed at all times. So many ways to win. I want to play more.

11 years pass and I play again to a new crowd. I was excited to show off this old gem but over a decade later, it just goes to prove that nostalgia aint what it used to be. I did remember the luck that presented itself with the colonies but I seemed oblivious to how swingy the expedition cards are and how they can be utterly useless if you lack the funds etc. Come game end I am utterly disappointed to realise that the luck element is so great. My '9' rating is totally out of proportion with more modern games that earn this high score and I must down grade to 7.5.  Perhaps time to sell on.

RATING: 10
Wonderful and deep game about developing spice trade in east. Auction system is clever for it is fast but need careful judgement about tile values and strategy adopted. Furthermore players can inflate or deflate currency with their actions and this is very important.
Played many times and still evalueting different strategies. Cards can seem too powerful, but my experience is that the game is very well balanced. There is not just one winning path.
Our group record is 51 points using ship/spice strategy. Note that in our latest games all 4 players score well above 40.

RATING: 7.5

RATING: 10
[Got it new and very cheap with damaged box on eBay.]
I love this game more and more every time I play it. The resource management feels a little like Puerto Rico but with even less time to accomplish your goals. I got this because I heard it works great for 2 as well as more. With 3 and 4 it's great--one of my favorites. I wasn't that crazy about the 2-player game. UPDATE: Just learned--after four plays--that I've been playing it wrong--I haven't been giving free spices with every plantation purchase. This might help mitigate the 2-player run-away leader issue. Will re-evaluate after next play.

UPDATE 2: Finally played again with 2 players. It was fine. I usually had less money than the other player, but I ended up winning by three points. Granted, I'd played 10 times before, and she'd never played. She probably should have beaten me by taking a few more of the auctions, but I think it would have been close, anyway. I did enjoy the 2-player game more this time.

And all of those traits are exactly what I want out of a euro.

RATING: 6
Okay game if you like economic games with lots of auctions, but (for me) it runs a bit too long for the amount of fun involved.  The auction mechanism is a little weird to wrap your head around at first, and there is a lot going on in terms of technology trees and combining actions to get yourself ahead in the game.  Novice players can "wreck" the game since they don't have an appreciation for the actual value of items (especially extra actions).  Additionally, there might be a slight runaway leader if someone gets too far ahead in cash.  Typical final scores are 30-50 points.

2012 update: Through no fault of your own, you can get really shafted with respect to your choice of tiles (or lack thereof) that you can put up for auction.  This can cause some sizeable money imbalances and "slow starts" for some players early in the game.

2014 update: I don't like it. No control over bad auction placements and people bidding too much to remain competitive or not bidding enough to put pressure on the leader.

** NOTE: I'm curious to test Double Dan's theory that the Exploration/Spice track combo (even with no discarding before or after drawing cards) is an unbeatable strategy w/4 players.  Also, Larry says that a variant rule for the Exploration cards is needed for the 1st edition.

RATING: 7.5
From all the games from Rüdiger Dorn I played. I easily find this his best. They don't come more cube-pushy euro than this, but when done right, I'm all up for it.

The stack of cylinders is a mechanic that seems to have transferred from Genoa, but now has its place in a much better setting. It's auctions for resources, combined with simple engine building, where the engine building itself is responsible for most of the victory points. Simple and clean.

Unlike Genoa not every resource or agreement is tradeable, but auctions are more ordinary, just using money. This, and the fact that auctions happen by default makes it suitable for a much broader audience.

Pretty much a classic.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Wow! My first time playing it and I was impressed! It reminded me of New Amsterdam and made me wonder why I'd ever choose that over Goa. It was simple and elegant without as many moving parts but with multiple viable strategies. It's an auction game where you bid for tiles X number of times during each of 8 rounds (where X is the # of players) over the game. Each tile either gives you additional space to put one of 5 different types of goods or gives you a one off (possibly recurring) bonus. After each set of auctions, players get 3 actions where they can advance tech tracks, get resources, or discover islands. At the end of the game, you get VPs for islands, tech advances, and a few other things. It was simple, elegant and I'd love to play it again. Why do we need new games when we have these old classics to play!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Es un juego de desarrollo. Cada jugador dispone de dos tableros. Uno en el que aparecen determiandos parámetros (que corresponden con acciones que escoger), y otro en el que ubicar losetas para almacenar mercancías y colocar losetas de conolización.
Cada turno tiene dos fases. Una en la que se colocan marcadores de secuencia de pujas y las pujas correspondientes por adjudicarse la loseta. La otra fase consiste en la utilización de tres acciones por cada uno de los jugadores. El tema se repite cuatro veces con el primer set de losetas y otras cuatro con el segundo set.

Al final se puntúa según el nivel de desarrollo que hayas obtenido en el panel, y según la cantidad de losetas de colonización.

Es un juego de bastante cálculo y sesudo, de los que a mí me gustan. Altamente recomendable y con reglas fáciles y asimilable por no jugones. Dura sobre la hora y media.

Lo compré en un pedido a juegamestore, aprovechando el vale entregado en las CLBSK2012. Lo recibí el 1/06/2012

Vendido el 05/01/2015 a Alberto MErino, a través de la BSK, en un lote de 13 juegos por 200€, con gastos de envío incluidos. Hice dos envíos. Este fue en el primer envío.

RATING: 7
Four player games can end with only a few points between first and last. The games are very tight. Sometimes too tight for the result to be meaningful. 

There is minimal player interaction. This is the quintessential multi-player solitaire euro of maximizing self efficiency. Nevertheless, this is a solid game. 

Rating update: 8 -> 7. After 6 games, I've confirmed Goa's Achilles' heal. The exploration card track is unbeatable. If someone has a set of 4 or 5 cards, then it breaks the game.

This is NOT a game with multiple equivalent paths to victory. There is a single path that dominates all others leading to poor replayability :-(

A real pity that such an otherwise well designed game is 'broken'.

Update: A New Expedition attempts to fix it. Based on one play, the card collecting seems to have been dampened. But the power of using Expedition cards is still there. It's less broken, though the power of that 4th track is still there.

RATING: 8.5
Whoa, this one's intense. So many things to keep track of, so many difficult choices, and you often only get one shot at them. What your partners choose has a direct impact on what you can do, and you always find yourself one or two actions away from doing what you really want. A very tight, tense game.

The auctions are painful, primarily because it's hard to gauge how much each tile is worth (since that value changes throughout the game depending on how much money people have). So during the bidding rounds, since you only have one shot, you may grossly over- or underbid on an item, which will give you a huge disadvantage that turn. One stupid mistake can make or break the game. I have made that stupid mistake three times. Sigh.

RATING: 7
I've played this now a number of times so it was high time I rated it. The bits are nice, the gameplay is relatively good. The auctioning is fun (nice mechanic), but I find the game a bit dry. It's a bit too complex for what it returns. This is a personal thing, but I find some of the euro games have too much complexity. It's almost as though the designer thought the game too simple and threw in yet another mechanic, or scoring mechanism, or resource.... 

I will certainly be playing Goa again, but I don't think it belongs in the top 25. One particular thing that I find somewhat annoying (along with many of the people I teach it to) is the different manners in which VP can be gained. This is usually a good thing, providing more than one avenue to success, but for Goa it seems there are too many and that they are tacked on. 

A difficult game to learn, but worth it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I didn't find it to be either as complex or as strategic as its reputation might suggest. Instead, I thought the play was tactical, with a focus on the short term. Tempo is very important, as is money management, but in general, the triangle number scoring paths leading to victory points are so closely balanced that the best overall path is to go for what you can maximize in the short term. I can understand players agonizing over wanting to do too many things but having too few actions to do them, but one answer to that is to buy the flag, tax with the extra action, and spend the money to buy the flag again (along with another extra action) in the next round. But for its length, I would put Goa clearly in the family game category.

RATING: 8
I've been holding back a bit rating this game due to the question about the possibly overpowered Exploration track, but now, playing with the apparently correct Hans im Gluck rules, I will ... I really like Goa. As these high-end games go, it really isn't that complicated and is pretty streamlined and, like Traders of Genoa, it innundates you with really hard choices. There is incredibly subtlety to the auction process. Then, do you go for balance, or focus on one or more tracks? Which tracks are important? It's great. And while there are certain truisms (you'll need to pick up some Exploration cards), there are a variety of ways to win - my most recent game I won and never took the Explorer action, I won by running up the Money track and acquiring everything I needed the bidding. Goa is reminicient of Traders of Genoa in the constant temptation to invest in all these cool things you can do, but like Traders, you need to balance long-term focus with taking advantage of short-term opportunities. It's definitely less of a meatgrinder than Traders though. For me, scores big over Puerto Rico and Princes of Florence in the huge variability of the auction phase, so you get a lot of variation in the course of play - you can't play pat strategies as you can in Puerto Rico, you have to keep adjusting.

RATING: 5
Problem comes in the action phase. If turns are taken in order, game takes forever; if taken simultaneously, the game loses any feel of interaction and competition. You are playing against the system.

Spectacular auction phase makes up for it somewhat. I love this auction, and wish this style would be copied more often. Great economic system.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I'm glad to hear that Goa is being reprinted, because this fine game deserves to be available to all board game geeks! I like the two different phases in the game, the auction and the action, and I think the combination of the two makes it an interesting game. I think you need to come up with a good plan/strategy to win, but you are never sure what you are going to win in the auction, so you need to be able to shift your focus too and make more tactical moves instead of strategic. I like that there are multiple paths to victory, and I really like the 5 different upgrade tracks! 

However, I have to mention the biggest negative part of the game: the founding colony action... This is a very random part of the game and can really screw you over! In one game I tried to found a colony and got 2 x 1 - after I tried to found a colony again, I got 2 x 1 again! While the other players got 3+2... And I ended up losing by 3 points, which I'm sure I could have managed to overcome if I didn't lose two actions in a row. I don't complain about losing, I don't have a problem with it if the winner played better than me, but when such a random element is a big part of it, it frustrates me.

I think it works quite well with 2 unlike many other games with auctions, though I prefer 3 and 4 with 4 being the sweet spot.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Comments after 3 plays:
the revised rules of GOA.
* Pay 1 less (rather than 1 more) when buying your own auction item.
* Player 1 starts with 5 gold (rather than 7)
* Money action tile gives 3 gold per turn (rather than 4)
* Three number 1 colony cards increased to number 2 (thus, the colony founding deck is more favorable)
* Third row on colonist column gives +4 to colony founding (rather than +3)
* Spice requirements reversed for the "refill plantations" and "gain cash" tracks.
* Special card "pay cash to increase technology level" is not an action modifier (and thus, effectively grants an extra action)
* New "2 colonist" B tile (this seems weak to me)
* New "exchange colonists for ships/spices" B tile (will allow spending those leftover colonists)

The game does not change that much.  I can see it is relatively easy to find colony.  Again, the first player is still too strong.  THe one who engages to card strategy sill overwhelms the game.
I wonder why the revised rules do not change the symbols matching score.  The game play will be twisted if everyone draw cards trying to match symbols in the last 2 rounds.  The game becomes dry and lost its original sense.

RATING: 7
Really good. In a game with this much to offer tactically, the expedition luck is a mixed bag. It gels nicely with the auctions, where what you draw will influence what you do (and if it doesn't, either you drew exactly the right cards or you're not maximizing your situation). In other words, I feel that the card mix is almost, but not quite, enough to allow for creative and effective reactions to random draws. It feels almost like there wants to be two decks, one for each epoch, with the more useful cards for each more heavily clustered.

Regardless, the auctions are vicious fun, and the action sequences are a series of delicious puzzles.  While in some games I don't care too much about the trackable 'hidden' information debate, in Goa the game improves dramatically when cash is open. This is a game that revolves around very important auctions dealing with very small numbers, and it seems contrary to the game's calculating atmosphere to have to make decisions based on fuzzy numbers.

Best with 2, almost as good with 4, kinda lame with 3 among experienced players (well-documented in Goa's forums).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Basically take the addictively fast play and variability of Puerto Rico, and then marry it to the tight decisions required in Kramer fare like Princes of Florence, and finally sprinkle on Dorn trademarks like the 'insane turn made possible by taking many extra actions' and you have Goa. 

Winning or losing this game feels *real*, you can usually sit back and point to something you did right or wrong, or even a few things, which contributed to the results, and you *want* to do that critical after-game analysis, the sure sign of a great game. 

All players are really trying to do, at the most basic level, is gain time, but you can do that in a wide variety of ways, extra actions are an obvious choice, but newbies often miss the fact that gaining 4 ships when your ship track is still on level 1 is effectively gaining you 4 actions, or that the oft-maligned Vice King is saving you a minimum of 1 action, but likely at least 2 or perhaps 3 (harvest - ships - the advancement action itself). I think the time saving crux is the root of the Expy track issue, as those cards are one of the only reliable ways to save yourself actions (not to mention pull away in the end game scoring) and if players aren't aware of the track and allow a double nutmeg to go on the block and to go too cheaply then the game can get out of whack in the early going.

If I have a knock on this game, it is that the paths to victory are not as balanced as they appear when you first sit down to the game, and the game has a certain flow that is rather samey from outing to outing. I think the Pepper/Nutmeg (Harvest plus Expy) combo is by far the most powerful one in the multi-player game, and is still a worthy one to pursue with two at the table. This is because the most powerful expedition cards are fueled by having a large harvest action ability (i.e.: Advance for only spice, Harvest in your choice of spice, boats, dudes) and having the extra spice around also allows one to go after the "Sac 6 spice for 4 points" tiles, which are a huge points boost that not everyone will be able to use. The ability to upgrade your whip (bigger taxation numbers) seems to be undervalued by the BGG population at large, but I think it is a crucial third option, and find it extremely powerful in the 2 player game even as a primary strategy. As long as everyone understands the value of the items, the game self-balances to some degree as it becomes a battle for scarce resources, but I do think it negatively impacts my impression of it slightly.

With all experienced hands this game has a lot interesting interactions going on though, and I love the choices involved in where to place the flag, how to bid in the auction, and so on. I still enjoy this game a lot. That said, as it currently sits, I like it, but I'm mildly burned out on it. It doesn't have quite enough variability to really demand even yearly play from me anymore, though it is certainly a cut above most of the stuff out there.


Update Summer 2008:
I've started making use of the variant cards that switch columns 2 and 3 so the pepper/nutmeg combo results in tax and cards instead of harvest and cards, and think that it improves the game noticeably. Since the most powerful cards in the Expy card deck are fueled by a big harvest, making it harder to get that two column combination makes for a more interesting, nuanced game where early game decisions are not as automatic as they were getting for me. I plan to play henceforth with the new cards, and I'm mildly interested in exploring the game again, though I doubt it will ever be a heavy play type of game for me again.

RATING: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Goa has taken quite a lot time and a few strategy guides to grow on me. I don't play it very often, but each time I take it out and play it, the game treats me like a good old friend full of fresh, interesting stories. That was different in the beginning, where I was just floundering about and not really paying attention to what would secure me points. In that sense, it has proven to have quite a steep learning curve for me. Ever since I managed to wrap my head aroung the the VP-gathering mechanisms, I could begin to actually play and make life really difficult for the other players: through the auctions. If people are complaining about a strategy being too dominant, what they mean is that they stink at this part of the game. If people are saying it is too much multiplayer solitaire, what they mean is that they've got their playing priorities reversed. Goa is an auction game with a few actions in between; look at it the other way at your own peril.

The second thing you need to learn to manage in this game is time. There's only 8 rounds of play, so make every action you take count, and don't waste your time (and money) on pointless auctions. Learning to balance the cash flow against the actions you must take with the added constraint of there being not enough time to do it in, wasn't an easy task. That is why the game offers many seamingly pointless tiles worth a few points: sometimes buying such a tile is a better alternative than going for the development chart and/or colonies. I had this terrible Pavlovian urge to play the game 'properly' and move all my cubes down the tracks in an orderly fashion: screw that. Points is what you're after, don't go wasting actions on items you won't be using before the end of the game.

The result is a rich and challenging game which, despite it sometimes rather mechanical feel and not particularly evocative theme (what do auctions have to do with spice trade?), offers plenty of possibilities to have you work out the best course of action. Inexperienced players have the disadvantage, but the 'reset' of the action tableau halfway and the fact that the game only takes 8 rounds means that even as an experienced player you cannot take things too easily. There will come a time when Goa no longer holds your interest because players tend to pick fruitful auction locations, but at the rate I am going, that will not be in the forseeable future.

RATING: 8.75
While the subject matter may be a little dull and tired, this game wins for the month because it combines a lot of things I like.  Beautiful artwork and cool pieces, many ways to score points, the ability to personalize your journey in a way that suits you, and a little bit of auctions.

Similar to Hansa Tuetonica, players have a board with tracks where they can improve their various action abilities - taking ships, money, colonists, and replenishing their supply of spices.  Everything works together, so focusing too much on any one thing will hurt you in the long run.

It took awhile to really get underneath all the required strategy, but I could see how I'll appreciate it more with future plays.  Dorn is becoming one of my favorite designers, and this classic is definitely a part of that.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Goa would have greatly benefited if its random elements had been completely excised, because they mar an otherwise interesting design. The N+1 auction structure and the constrained nature of what tiles can appear for sale ensures that auctions are varied and interesting, and rewards a high level of tactical flexibility. That in itself might have well have compensated for the lack of balance between the development tracks, in which level three cards are practically mandated, spices are far more useful than ships, and money and colonists largely superfluous. But Goa is all about small efficiencies; it takes multiple actions to make marginal point gains on your opponents. A single bad colonization attempt or a good run of expedition cards in the endgame, however, can completely overshadow that, and that's pretty galling.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Components: Very well-made, and despite all the different sorts of cards and action tiles, the information is layed out in a very intuitive, pictoral manner.

Experience: There is just so much to do in this game, but not enough time in which to do it; I never felt satisfied with any of my actions because they left other objectives incomplete. It doesn't give me the excitement or tension I'm looking for in a game (except in the auction phase).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Number of games played: 10 2-player games, 2 3-player games

The good

- Great auction system that works even for 2 players

- Nice strategic gameplay

- Nice engine building aspect

- Not a worker placement game for once.

- Equally enjoyable with 2, 3 and 4 players

- Reasonably low luck factor. Also, there is a (non-official) variant that almost totally eliminates the luck factor, requiring only a minor change to the rules. According to Luca Iennaco: let all expedition cards have "2" as "foundation number" rather than the printed (1, 2 or 3) value.

- Relatively short duration (approximately 2h with 2 players)


The bad

- Very thin theme

- Dull artwork

- Some counterintuitive rules (how exactly to obtain colonists?)

- No quick reference sheet


Conclusion: if there were a stronger theme and more interesting artwork, I'd probably put this game in my top 10.

RATING: 8
Initial rating based on only one play, so take this with a lot of salt (or pepper or nutmeg). Clever game that required balancing a lot of things simultaneously. Although it is a classic "lots to do, few actions" game, I never feel hamstrung: there are always good options that open up possibilities, so it's about choosing among several good options rather than the least bad of several bad options. Reasonably quick-playing strategic game that doesn't stall. Negative aspects are that it's not the most interactive game one could envision, it requires experience to know the value of tiles, making it difficult to mix experienced and new players, and expedition tiles are arguably a little overpowered.

RATING: 8
An auction game that plays great - maybe even best - with 2p. So much fun!

There is a ton of game outside of the auction phase, many deep decision points, but everything comes back to getting value in the auctions, and making the most of what you get. 

This is a classic that entirely stands the test of time.

RATING: 9
Solid, meaty game themed on the Portuguese attempts to corner the spice trade out of Goa.  Players auction off tiles (that do an assortment of things), then take actions (improve in shipping, harvesting, tax, expeditions or colonisation) in order to gain victory points.  Victory points are awarded in lots of different ways, the main sources being from your improvements and colonisation attempts.  Lots going on, but a game or two in, it becomes suprisingly fluid and feels very nice to play, with lots of ways to win.  The concept of the "grid" seen in Traders of Genoa returns here to determine what tiles are auctioned - works well.  Excellent with 2, 3 or 4 players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game just seems overwrought to me, but I concede it is a functional one where hardcore strategy fans will excel.

It has many many dozens of auctions over the course of the long game. You all slowly build up a rather same-y engine on your player mats. The amount of inputs required to get something accomplished is crazy. Eventually you build up technologies for growth, colonization, money income, etc.

It also features a bizarre phase where you select which upgrades will be auctioned off. There is a grid, and you must chain the tiles Boggle-style. Novel? Yes, but I fail to see what this phase accomplishes over any other selection mechanism; more weird experimentation at work I suppose.

Lastly the auctions rarely pay to the bank; the tile you selected pays to you (even more severe than in Traumfabrik where it pays out evenly between players). So the timing of the money flows can trip you up too. The money can even bounce between two rich players and leave others out in the cold.

Overall, it's an unbalanced and thinly-themed euro of pushing cubes around via auctions. Ultimately I find it too complicated for the payoff.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Goa. The game of Goa is divided into two sections an A and a B, and within each section there are four rounds, each consisting of an auction and an action selection phase. This very dry sentence encapsulates most the criticism of this game. Namely that the theme is a bit thin and the game is just mechanics, and that the mien is austere. However, I don't think this game is dry at all as the decision space that this game provides - through these mechanics - is so rich. This is achieved without the game being overly complex or long. There are a few smaller rules that can easily be missed from the rules, but these are small print. I have the German version of the game and was initially wary, however the English in the game is minimal, basically use once per round, use immediately and use at end of game, these are also coloured coded. In fact I don't think the German version added anything additional to the picture in terms of extra fiddle and the game ran as smooth as it would have done had this been the English. You'll still have to look up the iconography on the cards, but you would have had to do this anyway had the game been in English. 
 
As to the game: the auctions work extremely well (even with two, which is a massive plus in my book) and give the game a pleasing economic feel, which to me means one of being always conscious of what you can afford and conscious of efficiency and usually with the best move is selected rather than scripted. You are auctioning off tiles, and also the flag which gives you the ability to go first, which is actually quite important in this game - hence has a value. Money is done via cards in this game so that your solvency is hidden.  Once you've won certain tiles, which give you game abilities, resources and the ability to store them (the spice bags are lots of fun) or one off currencies (ships, colonists, money); this is followed by an action selection round where you aim for maximum efficiency on your three actions. The ability of which is determined by the level you have on each ability. A cursory glance at the player boards will get you up to speed. 

The game plays in just over an hour, offers a range of satisfying decision, plays well at two, plays smoothly without long set up, and most importantly for me doesn't really feel like any other game I have owned or played. The auctions are different to the ones in Powergrid, Ra etc. I'd never turn down a game. I'd play you now if you were game.

RATING: 5
I wanted to like this game, but it feels so much like multi-player solitaire, it's very dry and mechanical, and the theme is so obviously pasted on.  Other than bidding for tiles, your opponents hardly matter.  The bidding phase lacks any tension - if you don't get exactly what you want, you'll still get something else that's useful.  The final scores tend to be very close, not because of a tense struggle, but because everyone pretty much is doing the same exact thing - they will eventually build up colonies and progress on the development chart.  The only significant variable to the scores are the Expedition card sets, which often get drawn at the end of the game as everyone makes a last grab for VPs.  The problem with this, is that a player often wins because he got lucky enough to draw a set of matching symbols.  Goa is not a horrible game, but there are so many better games to play that provide more interaction and strategic tension.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Goa merges auctions with a spatial element with resource management and tech trees.  The first thing that struck me about the game was the auctions.  You start with a grid of tiles with various abilities.  The first player places the first player maker next to one of these tiles and places one of his auction markers on it.  Subsequent players will place their auction markers on tiles diagonally or orthogonally adjacent to the previously played marker.  After markers have been placed you'll have a 'once around' auction ala Ra (the person who is auctioning bids last) and if someone else wins the auction, they pay the auctioneer.  If the auctioneer wins, they pay the bank.  After that you'll use actions to gain resources (a number of types of spices, money, ships, and colonists), increase your tech and set up colonies.  This is a decent mid-weight euro that works with all player counts who I would recommend to any one that likes auctions and either tech trees and/or resource management.  The caveats are the randomness of the expedition cards, the tech trees don't seem balanced (though the new edition apparently fixes this), I worry about any game that allows you to pay someone else (because newbies can throw the game), and I find scoring a little too tight.

RATING: 6
[3 plays] Not sure if I'll like this better or worse when I play it more--could go either way.  It kinda has that auction-game annoyance where I never know how much to bid and feel like everything I do is wrong. I thought the 2nd game was a massacre and was stunned when I only lost by 2 points.  If I could play again before completely forgetting all about this game, I might be starting to get the hang of it.  
UPDATE: I thought I was getting crushed in my 3d game of Goa, only to end up winning.  While I was trying to figure out how that happened, it occurred to me that maybe I won the game for the same reasons I was starting to think I didn't like it.  Well, that would be too strong--I wouldn't say I dislike Goa, but I can identify some things I dislike about it.  I like games like Louis XIV and Macao where nothing is wasted.  Goa is the other kind of game, where the game ends and you have heaps of stuff that you spent blood, sweat, and tears to acquire, but which is now worthless.  This annoys me.  I felt like what happened in this third game is that I couldn't do what I was trying to do--and let it be noted that I NEVER had the flag, not one time--but because the relationship between what the game makes you feel like you should be doing and what actually scores points is so loose, the stuff I did in desperation when I couldn't do anything else ended up propelling me to victory.  Part of my edge was points from cards, which wasn't because I'd been trying to collect them or because I got lucky and got a lot of matching ones, but just because toward the end of the game I had the bad luck to get a bunch of cards I couldn't use for anything.

RATING: 7
This was a 9 for me at one point, but I dropped the rating due to a lack of inspiration to play it after about 10 plays.  I always do the same thing -- go down the right two tracks first and fish the deck on the last turn for matching symbols -- and I score about the same every game (and usually win). I'm curious about the changes to the new version, though, and would very much like to try it because there is a lot to like about this one. 

The auction is a wonderful exercise in brinksmanship and spatial positioning. The closed economy means the balance of power swings back and forth in a way that makes bidding decisions extra delicious. There’s a nice rhythm of having three actions every turn (well, plus the occasional extra one) that keeps the game humming along with planning and turn length. I hope to revisit the new version one day.

RATING: 6.5
I have to be honest and mark this down. It is a nice game, and heavy gamers may well get a lot from it. But it has one deep flaw for me, caused by the combination of two aspects: it's a progressive game (so the rich get richer), and it's an auction game (so the rich get richer). Having more money enables you to outbid players to get the resources you need to get more money and so on. I grant you, it is a nice set of mechanisms, the theme works, the spices and ships and the development chart all work, the scoring is nice. It's all there, but killed for me by the richer getting richer and no way to catch up. Screw up at the start and you stay screwed.

I may play this again, but my heart is against it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This has many more economic game elements in it than you'd think because of the once around auction where you pay the auctioneer.  There's a nice ebb and flow as money goes around to different players and inflation or deflation occurs as money flows into or out of the game.  The relative values of the tiles up for auction for each player make for some agonizing decisions regarding your bidding strategies.  There's a lot more going on than simply trying to buy what you want.  What really sets this game apart (and what makes it great for 2-player) is the once-around auction.  It's so easy for the person after you to just bid one ducat higher and take what you wanted but you don't want to overpay, so you have to get the price exactly right.  Fantastic.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Nice strategy game. Very strong.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[System Game, Auction/Action, Economic] {2P:8, 3P:7, 4P:8} "System" game in the same mold as many of the heavier German games. However, this game is unusually simple from a systems standpoint. I don't see any potential for it to develop a nasty, brutal side, which can happen when a game like Puerto Rico or Euphrat & Tigris is played defensively. Its demeanor is closer to Amun-Re and The Princes of Florence, but with more speed, less system complexity, and a similarly huge amount of fun. ***UPDATE: Still surprised by the speed at which this game can be played. 4P game completed in just over one hour, with the players taking actions rapidly, almost simultaneously. My take on the expedition track/cards so far: NOT broken as designed. Players who max the track are no more likely to win than the players who max the taxation track or who take a balanced approach. However, you can't ignore it any more than you can ignore building or shipping in Puerto Rico. As in many auction games, the system self-balances once the players catch on to the strategies taken by the group. So far, excellent game, but I'm still not thinking top shelf. No lower than 8, could go higher depending on the strategies that reveal themselves. My group tending to bid fairly high for the tiles, cash in the system turn-to-turn tends to be low, despite a lot of taxation track development all around. It's also not as complex as, say, Puerto Rico, but there is a lot going on and there are many options. One observation I have is with the scoring, which seems to be rather even all around, and is ultimately broken by either the bonus tiles or the expedition cards. Seems like you need that "edge" to win, and those are the only two sources of the "edge" as the track scores develop fairly evenly for all players (assuming they know what they're doing) and everyone makes sure that they get all four colonies. Am sure that there is much more to discover here. *** UPDATE: Increased rating due to the novel economics going on here. The auction round is substantially deeper than it initially seems, primarily due to the money supply fluctuations caused by people buying their own stuff and generating new cash via taxation, tiles and exploration. It's like the closed-economy Traumfabrik auction, with sporadic injection and draining of resources. This adds a very important memory element to the game, which is very interesting as it is maddening, as the ability to corner the market in auctioned resources is very powerful. Really cool, extremely fun, and worth the price of admission in and of itself. (It's also an inflation / deflation lesson in an economics jargon-free environment, always a good thing for younger players!)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
[b]Design:- [/b]:star::star::star::nostar::nostar:  [i]Economic engine. [/i]
[b]Execution:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::star:  [i]Auction uses innovative lot selection; development system.[/i]
[b]Experience:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::star:  [i]Extremely inyense; every action has to count.[/i]
[b]Presentation:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::nostar:  [i]Very good artwork, durable tiles and lovely wooden resources (spices).[/i]

This game is pretender to Puerto Rico's throne as the colonial trading game of choice; the depth is roughly similar to PR with a slightly more significant chance element in the form of the expedition cards.  Brilliantly put together game, seems to strike the right balance between choice and obligation as far as player actions are concerned and will probably surface more often than PR with our group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
All those comments calling Goa a real brain burner confuse me a little. There are admittedly quite a few options to consider and the cute non-linear auction serves to spice things up a little but when you concentrate on what you want to get done the game really seems to be rather more straightforward than I expected.

The major factor contributing to the players' level of uncertainty somewhat surprisingly does not seem to be the auction but the expedition cards. They can provide considerable bonuses, certainly enough so to call for a change of plan, but they can also be pretty useless under the circumstances. As such they do feel a little out of place at first in a game where every single action counts. However, if you think about it, they quite likely add a necessary fuzziness to a game that otherwise would be almost fully predictable.

I'm not quite sure how I feel about that. The expeditions do seem swingy, and I wish the problem would have been solved in a different way, but I cannot think of a better one.

RATING: 8
GOA hat schon so viel Jahre auf dem Buckel, das es anderen Spielen etwas erzählen kann. Zum Beispiel von einer Zeit, in der das Gamers Game Qualitätsniveau, das GOA erreicht, noch nicht so verbreitet war. Hinzu kommt, dass GOA bei aller deutlich zu erkennenden Handschrift von Rüdiger Dorn mit seiner öfter verwendeten Spurversteigerung wie auch in HÄNDLER VON GENUA und DIAMONDS CLUB ein recht eigenwilliger Vertreter ist, der sich vermutlich tatsächlich nur noch mit Kreationen von Herrn Dorn selbst vergleichen lässt. GOA ist eine Art gekonnt zusammen geklebtes Spiel aus zwei Spieltypen. So prägen zwei ganz unterschiedlichen Hälften das Gewürzspielwerk: einen interaktiven Versteigerungsteil und einer Phase, in der jeder autistisch an seinem Entwicklungstableau herumdoktert, wo man in 5 Spalten thematisch bemüht in niederländischem, orangefarbenem Kolonialdrang Schiffbau, Ernte, Steuergelder, Expedition und Koloniegründungen verbessern kann. GOA war in seinen jungen Jahren und ist bis heute kein wirklich eingängiges Spiel, das durchaus mit Verzögerung Klick machen kann. Das kann im blödesten Fall 12 Jahre dauern wie bei mir. Das Einzige, das die Zeit nicht so recht mitgemacht hat ist die grafische Einbettung von Oliver Freudenreich, die heute staubtrocken daherkommt. Betrachtet man GOA als heutiges Kiffer- und Aussteigerparadies, dann geht's schon wieder.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The interesting part of the game is the first phase when the auctions occur.  The players set what will be bid in during a control sequences and then each player auctions off an item where they either take the money from the top bid or undercut the market but pay the bank.

The second part of the game is player execution in turning resources into advancement on 6 types of tracks which have standard eurogame victory points at the eod of the game depending on how far down the track you've been able to move.

RATING: 8
Goa is quite good, one of my favorite economic-engine building game right now.  The once-around auctions are the heart of the game and are even better and more tension-filled than the "action" phase - which is also interesting.  There are many options, tough choices and trade-offs - almost too many for some people.  But the planning horizon is limited by the frequent auctions (which provide you with additional options) so perfect calculation is only available over a relatively short horizon.  Even with this, some players will suffer bouts of analysis paralysis if they are prone to it.

RATING: 8.5
[b]Goa[/b] [i]Rudiger Dörn 2004[/i] 
Goa is a superb design that works like a swiss watch but has some minor drawbacks. First it is a bit cold, second new players, as happens with most auction complex games, can be quite lost in the first games. However, it has a neat bidding system, fast and clean, and loaded with interactivity and tactical chocies, a system I do love. The new version contains dice (for the colonization tries), but the deck of cards works well for me. So yes, it is somewhat cold, and quite abstract, but given its quality I couldnt care less about it: a keeper and an excellent rotation game with an extremily clever and fast auction mechanic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Components: good board, good cards, good tiles
Complex: easy to learn
Weight: medium
Players: 3 or 4
Length: no more than 90 mins
Mechanics: bidding, tech tree development, hand management
Decisions: a lot, where to place the bids, where to bid, which path to follow...
Opinion: this is one of the first game entering my collection, and it is still a good one, no matter the time that has passed.
Lovely medium game to play to "relax" your grey cells, as they will be busy, but will not die :)
Probably is the best bid system I have seen out there, you have a grid of tiles, and you place your marker in one you want to be out for bidding (following adjacency with the ones previously selected by other players), and then make a one time bid in order, being the owner of the marker the last one. He has the option to accept the money bid or pay one more and take the tile (paying to the bank).
Then you have the rest of the game.... 8 turns, with 3 actions each. And, believe me, you do not have enough time to do all what you want. You have 5 techs to develop, and you can execute any of those paths.... should I go for ships (needed for develops), or should I go for spices (also needed for develops). No, I prefer going for money, used for bids, or for the amazing exploring cards....
The exploring cards, another thing I love about this game, small cards with 3 uses... you can use them for the bonus depicted, but you can keep them to the end game scoring, but, in the meantime, they are also used for the settlement of new colonies.
As already said, a medium weight game that I love since the first time I played, lot of tension and decisions, accurate length and lot of fun

RATING: 7.5
2005 acquisition.  Really want to play this one.  First play 2008.

The game has fantastic components and a very good insert for the various tiles and cards.  The board is very well made and functional.  There are a lot of things to remember and it took us a while to get through the rules.  Once we got going however, it wasn't too bad.  It is a nice auction, resource management game.  The players first bid for various tiles sequentially selected by the players.  Once all the chosen tiles have been auctioned off, each player then has 3 (or more) actions, to try to do a number of things to found a colony, harvest, tax the locals, build ships, go on expeditions, or advance on the development board.  There are a lots of things to consider and a lot of things you may want to do but there is often an agonisingly limited number of action you can perform.

Can't wait to play this again.

RATING: 8
Great game that centers around the auctioning of tiles from the board to the players. The theme is familiar, trading goods (spices, in this case). You grow the goods yourself and then ship them back home (Portugal) to advance yourself down the "tech tree".

The game moves quickly, has a fixed length of 8 rounds, and avoids multi-player-solitaire via the auction every round (requiring you to actually speak with your fellow players). A fun, solid euro game of low-to-medium difficulty.

RATING: 7
A tight and nasty economics game that is too unfocused to be truly great.

The auction is a stroke of genius. It works fabulously with two. Playing with open money makes for a vicious game.

Sadly the rest of the game is lacking. As nerve racking as the auctions can be, the rather tame action rounds boil down to perfunctory maintenance of a generic economic snowball.

The expedition cards are kind of hokey, and add an unwelcome dose of luck to an otherwise pristine game of pure skill. I'm not opposed to luck in games generally, it's just implemented poorly here.

I'm also disenchanted with the Euro trope of special-power combos, though I'm inclined to give Goa a break due to its age.

The game just doesn't quite gel together, with the auctions feeling somehow disconnected from the rest of the game—even though what you're bidding on, and thus the pricing, is intimately tied to the rest of the game. I'm not totally sure where the feeling of disconnection comes from, but it's definitely there.

The theme is totally—and I mean [i]totally[/i]—pasted on. We could be anywhere, trading anything. This isn't a problem for me really—I mostly don't care about theme. It's just so blatant an example of pasted-on theme that it bears mentioning.

It's a well-designed and [i]strange[/i] game, and is worth playing—if not owning—just for that.

RATING: 7
Tech trees? Resource procurement and conversion? Economy-building? And spatial auctions? Where do I sign up? This game has a lot of the traditional Euro tropes, blends them incredibly smoothly and adds a fine spice through the spatial mechanic for auctioning. We had already seen this mechanic in Traders of Genoa and would see it again in Louis XIV (together in what I would call Dorn's "trailing tower trilogy") but it seems most brilliant here with auctions as auctions are usually more discreet and less relational spatially (i.e., there isn't always a spatial relationship between what is auctioned). Above and beyond this, this game doesn't do anything screemingly innovative (even for its time) but it does it all so well (and still does it so well to this day).

While I agree that the the exploration cards can be powerful, the game balances out when it is understood by all players the degree to which they can be a make or break risk (which seems counter-intuitive in a game that otherwise plays like clockwork -- but this is a positive feature and not a flaw).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[Initial Impression: 9]
Just a fantastic auction and resource management game, made all the better that it works well with 2 players as well as 3 or 4. It's incredibly strategic, all the way from where to place the flag, to how much to pay in each auction, to which actions to take in which order. Even though you're working on your own advancement tracks, it is important to keep an eye on how your opponents are advancing - if they beat you down certain tracks, they will get the bonus card you were hoping for, and you can block them from successfully completing certain actions by buying tiles that they clearly need (or at least make them pay a lot at auction for them). Because the auction is once around, it is important to consider your bid carefully for each item, and don't make the mistake of allowing an opponent to scoop up a bargain. Be sure to always keep in mind that the aim is to earn victory points, which you'll primarily earn by carrying out actions - don't underestimate the value of free actions (including that won with the flag auction). Lots of people overvalue the expedition card track - it is very possible to win without relying on these cards (if your opponent is concentrating on the expedition track, you can block their access to extra actions and to the plantations they need to advance that track during the auction). It takes some time to learn all the tiles, how to value them, and to understand the interplay of the various development tracks, but once you learn it, Goa is an incredibly rich and strategy-laden experience.

RATING: 8
 $25.95 from fair play games
---

We have now given the game a few solid plays.  It definitely suffers from some complex rules and a fairly cumbersome setup process.  I don't know why, but we keep having a situation where I win the game by a decent margin, but only after 6 of the 8 rounds go by and we seem more or less even.

I suspect it is a stronger game with more than two players.

-----------
Only one play so far, and it was a bit of a struggle through some complex rules.

Challenges for a new player:
1. Auctioning is hard when you don't have a good sense of the relaive value of the various pieces.
2. Rulebook is not very well written... Encourage all new players to read the example on the last two pages before reading the actual rules.
3. Extra action card rules are buried on the 8th page or so.  very confusing.
4. Exploration cards cleverly adapt to three roles (end game points, opportunity to take special actions and random element when building colonies), but this mix of uses is very confusing at first.

That all said, the game seems to offer various ways to win.  You can focus on moving the grey cubes down the card for end game victory points, or you can focus on getting the most colonies, or you can focus on getting the tiles worth points directly.  But, to win, you need to do more than one thing, although not necessarilly all of these things.

Look forward to another try.  Feels like a mix of Alhambra and Settlers of Catan... (Auctioning like trading in catan, building settlements and multiple avenues to win like catan, tile style and payment system kind of like Alhambra.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Goa is an awesome auction game, with multiple paths to victory, and truly varying values to the various commodities being auctioned.

At the bottom of it, there are two rounds (A/B) each with 27 posssible choices, only 25 of which are visible, and only 20 of these will actually be auctioned. This ensures both some options, and variable commodities in games to keep the action fresh. (Note: In a 4-player game- all these choices are reduced with smaller numbers of players)

Each player chooses (1) tile to uaction, and the start player chooses a 2nd, making 5 total auctions.  These can provide plantations for harvest and advancement, or other usful commodities, such as Settlers or Ships, or vital additional Actions.  The winner of the 1st auction also gets to be start player in the next round, and takes an additional action - two potentially very useful assets.

After the auctions, each player gets (3) actions to harvest, advance, collect resources, or try to found colonies in the Spice Islands. There are rarely enough actions available, even with extra actions.

Players advance on (5) Technology Trees, each of which enhance your overall abilities, and capability to succeed in different ways.

I am very glad I won this in geeklist auction- it was truly a "game I didn't know I needed;" and I DID need this one.

Rated 7.5 based on a 1st solo 4-player session.

Raised to an 8 after the 2nd 4 player solo game- felt tight, and tremendous.

RATING: 8
(11/13/2010) Wow... this is an impressive design that has interesting choices, engaging theme, and is FUN to play.

It has a resource generation mechanic similar to Puerto Rico where plantations generate spice crops. Spice and shipping are needed to upgrade capabilities in five areas: more shipping, growing more spice, generating more cash, the ability to "explore" through exploration cards, and ability to claim colonies through greater number of colonists. VP are awarded on how much progress is made in each of the five areas.

The game consists of 8x rounds, where each round starts with a bidding phase, followed by 3x actions. The bidding is a truly novel mechanic where players place bid tokens in turn order from 1 to 5 on a 5x5 grid of tiles. The starting player determines where on the grid the next player may place a bid token on a tile (to temporarily "claim" it) adjacent to the start location, and subsequent players can place bid tokens adjacent to the last bid location. Players then bid once around for the next start player, and then for each of the remaining 4 tiles. The twist is that the winning bid is paid to the player who "claimed" the specific tile, sort of like Modern Art. The player who claimed the tile has the option to overbid the last player, where the bid is then paid to the bank. In this manner, players can generate income from the selling of tiles, which may represent all sorts of goodies: new plantations, action cards, ship cards, or additional colonists.

After bidding, all players take 3x actions in sequence, either making progress in one of the five areas, or getting more ship cards, planting (or harvesting, as the game says) more spice, extracting income, gaining exploration cards, or attempting to establish new colonies, which give more flexibility in spice production (Variety is the "spice" of life, and spice variety is crucial to making progress on the five areas).

There are other things to consider, such as attempting to gain "action" cards and exploration cards. Action cards are important, since normally there are only 24x actions per player in the entire game; additional actions are therefore advantageous. Exploration cards give random items, but may give a significant advantage at a critical moment in the game; they should not be ignored, as they are like "prestige" cards from Princes of Florence.

All in all, this is another solid game from Alea, which also brought you POF and PR. The rules are somewhat poorly organized, however (for example: how exploration cards are played and limited is explained in three different sections of the rulebook). Otherwise, everything is tight, and everything makes sense. A solid game, and I can't wait to play it again. Too bad it is OOP, but I hear a reprint is in the works! Initial rating a '9'.

Update (06/01/2012) 3 plays: First play after buying OOP copy from a friend. I had forgotten how nerve-wracking this game is, as it seems like every move I make is a mistake! Determining the optimum bid during the auctions is intense, as is the squeezing as much efficiency from the limited number of actions. I'll need several more plays to be comfortable. The initial buzz is gone, but it is settling to a solid '8'.

Update (02/02/2014) 5 plays: I've stayed away from this game for awhile, as I don't like how I feel after a game. This 5th play was no exception. I never have a clear idea of what certain items are worth during the initial auctions. After that, it is just a variation of collecting resources, converting them into "stuff", and slowing accumulating VP along the way. There are so many ways to go about this, but there is so little time. Thus, every action card is precious. Winning the "flag" and extra action is significant, as the player has the opportunity to profit off of two bids next turn. There is still a period of unfamiliarity that I am wading through. There is a great game here, but I still cannot figure it out. Holding at an "8".

RATING: 7
I'm not sure about this one yet, but I certainly would like to play again. I found myself getting annoyed at the once-around auctions, but know that it probably takes 30-60 minutes off the play time as opposed to open auctions (and adds a depth of strategy). It also has the old style euro mechanic of draw a few cards and keep one or two of them which I never really like.

I really enjoy the tactical nature of the game and how your strategy has to evolve around what tiles you win during the first few rounds. The fly by the seat of your pants nature of the beginning of the game is really exciting. I'm worried that this sense burns out toward the end of the game though.

RATING: 6
Last Played: 2013-2015

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
AA起始設置:
1.玩家領取5個競標標誌(4人5個，3人4個)放置殖民圖板上，發展版圖有五個科技軌第一個都放置一個灰色token，4條船和2個殖民者，起始玩家5元與起拍標誌，其他玩家10元。
2.中央版圖放置25片拍賣板塊，移除4片(其中不能有兩片田地以上)，殖民板塊洗混面朝上放置。探險卡洗混朝下放置。
BB行動階段:
1.放置拍賣標記:
   從起始玩家順時針開始放置拍賣標記，第一輪首位一定要從外側開始，後續玩家必須要放置在9宮格區域內。若無法以此方塊放置，可以選擇空降。
2.拍賣板塊:
  採一輪競標，得標者錢給主拍人，主拍人為最後出價可以少支付1塊，拍賣首家板塊後得到首家標示與1張額外行動卡。
3.玩家行動:
從拍到首家板塊開始總共執行三次主要行道和一次額外行動階段。
1.升級發展板塊: 支付香料與和香料數量相同船隻卡升級
   一次只能升級一種，當所有發展方塊都發展到第二層以下，可以獲得
  一張額外行動卡，第三、四層亦如此；當圖板上任一排首先發展至第
 四層可以獲得一張探險牌。
2.獲得發展版圖上資源:
***第四排為抽取探險卡，左邊為可抽取數右邊為手牌上限，注意抽取牌庫時，抽取數量不能超過手牌上限數量，抽取前可以牌先打出使用。
第五排為建立殖民地，需求數量如殖民版圖數字所示(6/8/10/12)，發展版圖上為可提供基本殖民數+探險牌堆收兩張+手中打出。可以不用按照順序殖民，不能同名。失敗得到一位殖民牌。
****獲得作物板塊跟殖民板塊第一次都會直接有香料產出。
**三次行動結束後額外行動牌玩家可以打出依序執行，每輪最多保留一張。
CC結束計分:
1.各排發展版圖分數(如左邊所示0/1/3/6/10)
2.殖民地分數(1/3/6/10)，農地下方分數
3.探險牌右下方計分(看相同數量 1/3/6/10/15/20)
4.金錢最多3分，拍賣板塊額外分數
*****探險牌 上半部分為行動區域，下半部分左邊為殖民者判別右邊為
最後計分，若上半行動區域印有紅色A字樣，必須搭配行動階段使用。
!!特殊卡片: 1.升級用錢代替，一次升一階 2.行動階段執行領取香料，可以自由搭配香料或船或殖民者。3.行動階段執行殖民行動多翻一張卡
******拍賣板塊: 
1.藍色板塊其中有立即使用板塊，橘色板塊為每輪提供資源板塊，
紫色板塊有最後給分以及任意食客可以丟棄香料換分最後計分
2.藍色一次性板塊:
攝朕王(Vice King):發展版圖上最慢一排免費升級一次
交易板塊(Trade):可以在自己行動階段用他交換一個拍賣版圖上來使用，此板塊之後可以繼續再被拍賣使用。
供應板塊(Supply):抽取兩張探險牌，得到一種該數量的香料/船/殖民。
間諜板塊(Espionage):選擇對手的能力執行，除了探險牌抽取
額外收穫:填滿三個田地或殖民地。
棄牌板塊: 選擇A或B設置遊戲時所棄置的剩下各四片板塊一塊執行。

RATING: 8
An auction plus engine game. Most of the interest lies in the auction: deciding where to place the flag and which item to offer for sale. After that, it's really just a game of eking out small advantages to get slightly ahead in the scoring, although there is a bit of luck in founding colonies and which exploration cards you get. It's better than Princes of Florence in that auction items are much closer in value, there's no 'must have' item like PoF's jester, but the connection between theme and mechanism is weaker than in Puerto Rico. It's a little lacking in tension or excitement, there's no scoring track or VP chit pile to give a sense of who's ahead, but this is still much better than more recent Euros and playing it again recently was a breath of fresh air.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Excellent. I'd describe it as "efficient".. each action you take must benefit you as much as possible, BOTH in the long and the short term, and you have very few actions allowed to you.  Since value is very dynamic due to the tech tracks and when things come up, there is always some question as to what things are worth. Lots to think about here. 

After several plays it's become a little more manageable to me and I'm just liking it better and better. Even if you are pretty flush with resources, there's always something more you need to attend to to get where you need to be.

[ENG]

Resource management that can be explained in less than twenty minutes. Good for people who are looking for more after a gateway game. The main goal is developing (in a quite abstract way) a spice export business in the colonial portuguese era of Goa´s island. It has a bit of "solitaire multiplayer" game balanced by the interactive auction phase,that gives a bit of non-aggresive interaction. There are a lot of things to do but you dont have enough money or time to do it. Further, the game is good for 2 players. CONS: You might need more than one game to understand the whole mechanism. Pasted theme. Luck (in card variety form).

RATING: 9
This reminds me of Puerto Rico, Princes of Florence and RA actually. Why RA, the importance of knowing the worth of what items are up for bid, also the items are worth different amounts to different people, these two things stand out for me plus you have the once around type of auctions as well. The multiplayer solitarie complaint is slightly overrated, lots of interaction in the auctions and you need to be watching what each individual is doing as well, kinda like PR. The fact this scales so well from 2 - 4 is another big bonus in my book. Another big thing that stands out in this game is there are so many things you need to do and never enough actions to do them, all this and the multiple paths giving you lots of strategies to explore really make it a great game.

RATING: 8
First off, I'll have to say that on the surface Goa has everything I enjoy in a heavy German game: Auctions, many roads to victory, turn angst, and a cool theme to boot. For a German game, it's quite complex (even compared with other heavyweights) and feels most of all like a mix of Puerto Rico, Amun-Re and Princes of Florence. Like Princes, every round consists of auctions for common resources, followed by individual actions by each player. "Money" (ie. spendable resources) come in many flavors, including dubloons, colonists, ships, and spice sacks. Add to this a healthy handful of different tiles, a deck of many different cards, and about 6 or 7 different ways to score points at the end of the game, and you've got an incredibly meaty game.

Despite my enthusiasm for "those kinds" of games, I am not ecstatic about Goa: The theme (while cool) is almost non-existant and Goa really feels abstract in a way Puerto Rico or Amun-Re don't. In addition, a few of the more random game mechanics (drawing cards to colonize, getting VP for sets of expedition cards at the end) don't fit well with the rest of the game's uber-heavy feel.

So overall, I find Goa to be a fine game, if lacking a bit of the soul which makes other heavyweights (eg. Puerto Rico, Amun-Re, E&T) the classics they are.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Brilliant.

Auctions create tension - will player B select what he knows I need because he knows I will pay him a lot of money for it, or would he prefer to screw me over?

Auction tile placements create tension.

Building your engine isn't overly difficult, but there's enough to it that you need to take risks sometimes, make a few small reaches if you're going to be able to win the game.  It takes some planning to get there - usually 2/3 of my actions are pretty well scripted, and the third one can usually be used on a whim.

Triangular scoring rewards specialization, but specialization makes it harder to advance, so the best result seems to be towards slight specialization to maximize score, but some balance to maximize efficiency.  This helps games play out very differently.

RATING: 9
I love anything with a tech tree.  Goa is in part an auction game, and the other part is an optimization exercise that provides the basis for evaluating the auctioned items.  The genius of the game is the closed economy, with limited opportunities to inflate or deflate the currency.  If there is a secret to playing Goa well, it is keeping track of the inflationary/deflationary events.  A player who is tracking those events well will score great deals in the marketplace and ride those deals to a decisive advantage in the final scoring.

Requires a few plays to really enjoy, as it takes a while to figure out how to value tiles properly.  This is one of those games that plays far better if all players are near the same experience level, which isn't always possible.  In fact the newbie binding seems so deterministic that I'm much less willing than I used to be to play with a newbie whom I don't think will be interested in making it up the learning curve eventually.

I do wish the cards weren't so powerful, though of course an auction game does self-balance to an extent.

RATING: 6
Yet another Euro with a whole bunch of processes intertwined with an auction phase. The spatial aspect of the auction (you can only put up an item that is adjacent to the previously auctioned tile) is a twist I hadn't seen before. 

Not much in the way of thrills, this is definitely a game played largely in silence. One game isn't nearly enough to explore all the possible strategies, but I know my tastes well enough to file this one under "Not My Style." Still, I'd play it again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A game rife with challenging decisions. I think of this as the book-definition of a Eurogame. The only luck is in the tile draw, but the bidding mitigates it. Every choice affects every subsequent choice, as each game element intricately depends on one another for advancement. Every turn you are left wanting to do more than you are allowed. I'll play this anytime.

My only criticism would be that the mechanics are almost TOO obvious, which can make the game feel a little mechanical at times, but it doesn't detract from my enjoyment.
