RATING: 7
Esperaba más de este juego.

Tiene dos fases distintas, la fase de subasta y la fase de acciones. Los jugadores pujan por las losetas en la fase de subasta y colocan las que ganan en sus tableros. En esta fase hay un poco de interactividad.

Por otro lado, en la fase de acciones cada jugador tiene 3 acciones, basadas en el progreso de su tablero. Y aquí está el problema, me temo. Este juego te obliga a estar muy centrado en tu propio tablero, y en la segunda fase no hay interactividad.

Es un juego muy parecido a Los Principes de Florencia, pero no tan bueno como éste.
[color=green]
LAST PLAY: 2010
[/color][color=blue]
I was expecting more from this game.

It has two different phases, one auction phase and one action phase. Players bid for tiles in the auction phase, putting their won tiles at their own board. There is a little bit of interactivity in this phase.

On the other hand, in the action phase each player has 3 actions, based on the progress of their own boards. And here is the problem, I guess. This game forces you to be very focus on your own board, and in the second phase there is no interactivity.

It is a game very similar to The Princes of Florence, but not so good as it.[/color]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Goa is always a little anticlimactic. The subtleties and unique qualities of the auction are enough to set Goa apart - way apart. But at the end of the game I always score within a narrow point range, and even if my tech trees vary a bit, the end product feels too similar. Goa has the intense pressure for action efficiency that sours me on Agricola, but at least it keeps the rhythm even. Goa may also be one of the most unforgiving games I can think of. Making a mistake feels very rigid, as though your best possible outcome is now irrevocably worse than it was before you made the mistake.  

Update: I love the auction, but sometimes the game around it just bugs me. Managing 5 generic bottlenecks with 24 actions can feel tedious, but then, there are sessions where the cards and inflation add a sort of outrageous giddy quality to it all, which is weird. On the one hand, the cards often feel too random. On the other hand, the game badly needs them. 

2nd Update: I've decided that it's not just the excellent auction but the expedition cards as well that give this game substance. Maybe that makes me extraordinarily slow to catch on. The actual infrastructure part of the game is just plain bad on its own. A sizable number of Goa players object to the expedition cards as overpowered - essentially creating a single path to victory. I'd go them one further and say that leaning on the cards is not only crucial to success to required for the game to be any fun. The cards give you tangible short term goals, a clear head during the auctions, and much needed shortcuts as you improve your score. So the question is: will skilled players be following similar paths every game, and if so, does it matter? A score of 42-60 can win (usually, more like 45-50). Almost every score in that range will have four of six (counting the colonies) infrastructure tracks maxed out. So it's not really a question of which tracks you go into, but in which order. I think the even-handed approach is largely doomed to failure. Instead, an expedition foothold at level two seems like the standard opening. From there, perhaps anything is viable, but the shipping and taxes tracks seem significantly weaker than the harvest and colony tracks. The harvest track combines extremely well with the expedition track, because you can leverage it a couple of different ways for massive upgrades, sometimes using it to make up for a neglected shipping track. There is no way to do the same thing with the shipping track. You can also use the harvest/expedition combo for one or two large influxes of cash, making the tax track questionable. I suppose you could go into the tax track to double up on money and then buy your way up the colonize track. Either way, harvest/expedition seems flat-out better than any other core strategy. 

Despite that, there are still couple of game layers that can be played any number of ways. Extra actions? Mission VPs? Most Money? Espionage? Is the Auction enough to allow for different playstyles? Would the game be better if taxes/shipping were stronger and if bad luck on colonizing weren't as jarring? Or would it just feel easier? And if it just felt easier does that mean the game isn't interactive enough? I don't have the answers to these questions. At times they feel largely theoretical... the occasional game of Goa can be both lively and satisfying.

RATING: 7.5
There is an auction phase in which you can buy various cards. Many of these are used to produce goods on that player's plantation board. Another option is to colonize which also builds up the plantation. Then players have a chance to take three actions. Items from the plantation can be used to advance any of five tracks. Also ship cards are needed to advance along these tracks. As the tracks advance more resources can be generated such as ship cards by using an action. The victory points are mainly from how far advanced are the tracks and how far along are the colonized plantations.

RATING: 6

As there's no online implementation the question probably won't be answered.

In Goa, the exploration cards seem to be one strategy which must be followed and colonies are mandatory as well. Focussing on money on the other hand is suboptimal at best. There's enough randomness in the exploration cards and the establishment of colonies as well as player interaction in the bidding phase to add interplay variety but at the same time the potential for frustration is raised by the random factor.
When it comes to classic gamers eurogames I rather go for the Knizia ones.

RATING: 8
Proper rule on Expedition track is a MUST or game fractures. The auction is why Goa>PoF. In PoF, ppl have made reasonable estimations as to how much a jester is worth on round 3. This type of specialized knowledge is a death knell (for me). In Goa, no such analysis may be done since it depends on how inflationary (money track actions, payments to other players) or deflationary (payments to the bank) the particular game has been. Advancing the Expedition track makes that player's game chaotic, but it can be a powerful option. The randomness of successfully founding a colony is not infrequently unbalancing in a given game... hence, not a 9.

RATING: 9
A building game.

Good: Wonderful and deep strategy game. Many different options and strategies to choose from. Each player has a matrix board with 6 columns of different strategies that can be advanced in any order. Rules are some of the simplest I have ever seen for a heavy strategy game.

Bad: Takes 3 hours. However, typically you are so immersed in the game that the time just flies by. I am not a fan of the expedition cards, as I feel they bring too much luck into the game.
The bidding can create problems - it takes time to develop a feel for the value of the cards. Therefore anyone newer to the game can accidentally give things away to another player.

RATING: 9
A-4
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Combination of an auction, action and exploration game. So many choices and options ... maximising some of the tracks or trying a balanced approach. There are many roads to victory, but don't forget the expedition cards as these might just make the difference in the end. ***** Reminder: mistakes made during first games: (a) During a turn (action + extra actions via blue tiles) only one expedition card can be played. (b) If the founding of a colony is unsuccessful, then you get one colonist - not an extra action card. (c) If you develop all 5 columns to the second row, then you get an action card (that's why it is mentioned next to the colonist column). Same for the next rows. (d) The expedition cards with an A enhance an action and have to be played before - NOT during - the action (e.g. the extra expedition card, 3 instead of 2, when founding a colony). (e) The viceroy blue tile gives a free upgrade for the least progressed track. (f) The blue tiles (e.g. espionage) should be played in addition to a normal action (either before or after).

RATING: 9
If, on first hearing, the rules sound a overly complex and too fiddly, don't let it stop you and give Goa a chance: you won't have to regret it. This is a beautifully crafted game and it flows surprisingly well. Many different tactics can be used to win, some involving more luck than others - which can be a bit frustrating when your carefully elaborated plan is ruined by some luckard with his expedition cards. Goa is not a beginner's game, its theme is dry and artificial but if you enjoy a well crafted and well oiled gameplay, then you should definitely try it out. But before you do, one word of advice: make sure you understand scoring well!

RATING: 8
UPDATE: Has anyone played with the 5 player expansion?

Embarassingly enough, we only just played our first game at GenCon. This is a great game and even though the player with all the experience won, the game was tight with scores of 36-32-30-27. 

The auction mech is greatly enhanced by the selection of tiles off the Flag location - and you have essentially the ability - money allowing - to take the one you chose. Then choosing which of your "techs" to advance also provides a wealth of choices in pursuing a strategy.

We liked enough order while still at the Con and look forward to becoming something of a regular game around here.

Will likely try with fewer players but absent rules that alter the auction process, it would appear that a 2 player game would lose a lot.

RATING: 9
Another highly tentative rating (see High Society) after 2 plays.  I have to admit I'm having a hard time getting my head wrapped around everything that's going on in this game, so I can't give it a real firm assessment yet, but I definitely want to play again.

UPDATE: After 3 more plays, I am raising this rating to a 9, The game offers great depth, and is a strong intellectual challenge of trying to balance numerous factors.  I'm not sure if it will stay at 9 forever - the replayability probably isn't infinite - but I definitely enjoy playing it a lot right now, and expect to for the foreseeable future.

UPDATE: Goa's replayability is much better than I had feared. Of course, some of that may have to do with how bad I am at the game, but, nevertheless, I am now 16 plays along and still eager to play several times more. The visible gears, and the clunkiness and asymmetry of the colonization track keep it from being a 10, but I doubt that I will ever really regret playing.

RATING: 9.5
[microbadge=5]

Goa is a unique game, seemingly highly abstracted and a mess of mechanics thrown together, but in fact is a neatly balanced combination of market fluctuation and skill trees.  Some of the aspects might seem randomly included (or randomly applied; read: Expedition Cards), but correctly assessing what is of a good value not just to you but to every other player as well puts this game in the ranks of Power Grid as far as highly-interactive Euros with no preset strategy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
My initial plays were with my wife, 2 player. I enjoyed the "cat and mouse"  game in the auction and liked the openness of the tech tree. I rated it an 8.5.

Then I finally got to play a 4 player game with my euro gamer friends and one of the players "broke" the game. He was able to acquire everything in the game. From set collections, to colonies to maxing out the tech tree. He maximized his money earlier, then expedition card tech and continuously focused on those cards. By the second last round, there was really nothing else for him to do except cycle the expedition cards to try and score more sets. The other players at the table should have been more competitive and recognized the power of this strategy earlier but at the same time, the fact that someone can "maximize" their score during a game doesn't sit that well with me.

But worse, is that in the future, whenever I think about playing this game, I'm going to be aware of this strong strategy. As a game that comes out once or twice a year, most players won't be familiar with that strategy or maybe even remember it and especially with my wife, my main player for this game, it basically isn't going to work anymore as I'm not going to power game.

RATING: 7
2-4p, 90+m.  Highly rated--some people prefer this to Puerto Rico, the latter handling 3-5 players.  I also prefer it to PR, though both tend to be a bit longer than I prefer.  Goa's limited actions per turn becomes agonizing towards the end as you realize you may be only 1 or 2 actions away from overtaking an opponent.  I must say that the end-game bonus for the matching island names on the cards-in-hand is a rather arbitrarily lucky stroke.  It allows someone who appears weak on their mat position to actually win, which can seem anti-climactic and random.

RATING: 9
Expedition card screwups:

1) End-game symbol scoring is based on matching symbols, not non-matching symbols!

2) Hand limit only applies when taking the expedition column action, not when winning an expedition tile, etc.

I bought the game 06/30/04. I used the expedition card hand limit rules as written in my Rio Grande rulebook for two years, since the arguement was never resolved as to why they differed from the German rules and what the designer said. At the time Jay Tummelson of RGG swore he changed nothing. And strangely/interestingly his "version" matched the Hans im Gluck FAQ. Very weird. 

Two years went by. Now (July 2006) RGG has issued a second edition. And I see the expedition card hand limit rules now coincide with the German rules. Yet Jay Tummelson never did admit they were wrong in the first place. They were just quietly changed. Whatever. Either way its still a great game.

As of 09/06/17 I've played using my set nine times.

Played as: 3,4 player

RATING: 1
I am aware that I am against the overwhelming majority here.
But this game is not only WAY too abstract for my taste, it also lacks (almost) any means of interaction, with the exception of that awfully awkward auction.
Furthermore I fail to see how the game-mechanics are related to what the game is supposed to simulate.
This is a game about game mechanics, not about building a colonial enterprise.
Thus, if you like abstract games with low interaction and pointracing, this will be your game. Otherwise, if you prefer sophisticated simulations with a high amount of flavour and interaction:
Beware!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[b]Original Comment (Better for New Players)[/b]

Goa is about on par with Agricola as a "resource management game that I actually like." Both do a good job of mixing a light spatial element with the more prominent process of converting various colorful wooden bits, cards, money, etc. into other colorful wooden bits, cards, money, and ultimately victory points. Both have a completely solitaire "tableau" area that players develop over the course of the game and a central area where the inter-player contests occur, driven by worker placement in Agricola and auctions in Goa. Agricola has the advantages of a more integrated theme and replayability from the cards, but Goa's actually the game that has a bit more of my interest for now.

I had the fortune of finally playing this with three recently, and I must say I decidedly prefer Goa--and perhaps all such games--with just two players. My three-player game took two hours, some bits of which were slightly more interesting than what you'd get with just two, but not really worth the extra hour. At that length, I also become more aware that I'm playing a "spreadsheet" game, as my patience begins to wear a bit thin. With two, Goa takes us only an hour but provides enough to think about that I'm engaged the entire time.
____________

[b]Updated Comment (after over a dozen plays)[/b]

Pretend for a moment that money was removed from Goa. Players would just pick tiles they wanted (instead of picking tiles to be auctioned), as long as those tiles were adjacent to the previous pick. Essentially that would be a worker placement game in which you're competing for "actions" (tiles) to help you develop your individual tableau. Sound familiar? As with all these other games, enough plays will probably find a somewhat optimal path. In this case, it seems to be getting to the second row in all five columns (literally or virtually via an orange tile) and then pounding down the Expedition track.

But Goa [i]does[/i] have money, used in auctions for tiles and brought into the game via player actions. This layer is to me what makes Goa different from any other game I've played and is therefore its most interesting aspect. Moreso than the randomized tiles, the money management aspect of the game is really where the variability comes in. New players will undervalue everything. Old players will have preferences you need to accommodate in your bidding. You constantly have to be considering how much money is in the game, how much everyone else has, and so on. While this additional layer got Goa past my "JASE" filter, now that I've seen it for what it is, I'm not sure how excited I am about it. Take the money management away, and you've got Agricola (Family Rules). Take the tableau development away, and you've got Modern Art. I don't think I really need a mashup of the two.

RATING: 7
Saw a copy a few days after playing it with the YVRBGG group, and bought a copy because of its relatively hard-to-find reputation. A Fun game of production and resource management, with many paths to victory, each of which really has to be mixed with at least one other to produce a viable winning strategy, producing dozens of combinations. Also relatively forgiving of switching strategy midgame. The oft-maligned colonist cards certainly add a level of adrenaline that most Euros lack, despite the disappointment when they work against you.

RATING: 7.25
This game is right in my wife's & my wheelhouse -- Euro, mid-level complexity, little conflict, some optimization to be done. And the game did not disappoint -- we both enjoyed it! I'm a little curious about long-term replayability, as it felt like throughout the game, both of us were generally following the same pattern of which track to upgrade and when. But I'm certainly not going to worry about it until after a few more plays.

One nice thing about the game is that the turns are very straightforward. You don't really have to worry a lot about following a specific procedure, upkeep of certain things, etc. It's simply: 1) place your auction markers; 2) auction; 3) take 3 actions; 4) repeat. As a result, the game moved at a pretty quick pace! I appreciate games like this because I find that in other games where there's some between-round upkeep (like Through the Ages: A Story of Civilization or Agricola), I have to break my concentration more than I'd like just for game maintenance. I like a game where maintenance is minimal.

AP rating: 9

Purchased: 2006
Expansions recommended: None available
Age Range: 8+

Reason Purchased: Geek analysis

Cons: replayability does suffer due to a sameness of each game, playing more than a few times a week can cause burnout (solo exempted).  An expansion to the game would be welcomed.

Recommendation: worthy purchase, game has a nice flow and plays quick.  Decisions are not overly taxing and it is possible to come from behind to win it in the end 
Replayability: moderate
Game value (play/cost ratio): very high

RATING: 7
In 16th century Goa, players first bid on a number of tiles on a 5 X 5 gird. The number of tiles auctioned is equal to the number of players and the first player gets to place a flag which determines which tile is auctioned first, and the flag itself which is bid on is worth an extra action. Players place their player marker to determine which tile should be auctioned off, and bidding is once round, with the money going to the owner of the player marker. 

After the bidding round each player has 3 actions. Each player has 2 player mats - one mat tracks the colonies they have founded, the other has 5 different tracks to progress: 1) more ships, 2) produce more gems, 3) more money, 4) expedition cards, 5) bonus in founding colonies. 

Players earn pts for how far they progress down the tracks, the number of colonies they have founded, sets of cards and other tiles won from the auction. 

It's a very interesting game and it grows on you. However there is one flaw, and that to be successful, you are forced to go down the expedition card track. The cards in the game are typically better than actions you can take on other tracks. There really is only one way to victory, so the game doesn't have a lot of variety in terms of strategy. I like the auctioning mechanism and the different tracks to progress on, so I sort of wish the expedition card track could be balanced out. The game was published in 2004, and there was a release in 2012 to try to fix some of the imbalance, but I'm not sure that was achieved. It's an engaging game, tense and full of angst, so it's too bad there aren't too many alternatives to your strategy.

Another minor quibble I have with the game is that two people can collude to pay  a high price to each other for the flag, thus gaining an extra action over their opponents.

It's a good game to try and explore for a while, as it has a lot of depth.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A great game in the style of Princes of Florence or St. Petersburg; that is, a game where a lot of small efficient moves will determine the winner. Progress is steady and measured, with many interesting decisions for the players to make, due to the marvellously designed interlocking subsystems used in the game.  However, it's also a game style that lacks drama -- there are no surprise moments or grand gestures, so if what you want is [i]excitement[/i], you'd better look elsewhere. Thematically, Goa is similar to Puerto Rico, but structurally more like PoF.  If you  like Princes and do well at it, you'll enjoy this one too.  

Unlike Princes, this plays well with fewer players, so it's worth owning both games.  However, Princes is less fiddly, more strategic, and has a more dynamic auction phase, which makes it a bit better game overall.  Still, Goa is an excellent purchase if this style of game appeals to you and you are looking for something for 3-4 players. It certainly appeals to me! :meeple:

RATING: 8
My thoughts on this game have changed more than any other in such a short period of time. At first I thought it was an incredibly deep game, but I had some concerns over the luck aspects - particularly the symbol scoring of the expedition cards. A few more plays convinced me that this wasn't nearly the problem I thought it was since the cards were too valuable to collect. Now, after about 10 games, my opinion is heading downward. The reason is because of those darned expedition cards. In my mind, the only viable strategy is to invest heavily in gaining the ability to draw and hold more expedition cards. All other courses of action are just too time consuming based on the limited number of actions you get. We went from the winner scoring in the low 40s to now when the winning scores are near 60. The only way to compete is through the cards. That said, the luck factor shines through in full force, both in who gets the "good" cards (all cards are good, but not all cards are equally good to all people - if the right cards come up for you, you'll do well... otherwise you'll struggle... though you will STILL beat those that try an approach OTHER than using the cards) and who gets the matching symbols at the end. I still enjoy playing the game, and that's the most important factor... maybe I'll find other ways to win during future plays!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Not being particularly innovative (managing six or seven things at once has pretty much become a fixture in German games nowadays), Goa is nevertheless one of the games where the wealth of included mechanisms (positional play, auctioning, resource management, action cards, engine building, a pinch of luck) have been interwoven in the most tasteful fashion. Players auctioning for tiles placed on a board before each turn is a quite ingenious addition to the usual "four actions in a round and four rounds in a phase" routine, and the rather complex network of dependencies between all resources to have them developed as evenly as possible for optimum results has been faultlessly balanced.

The eclectic amount of the elements comprised in Goa means that it takes a bit longer to understand it than other games with a comparable or even higher level of difficulty (as the ever so present Caylus). The other side of it is that Goa is a more varied game, where both the setup and the development of each phase can lead to unexpected turns more easily. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5

Il gioco è complesso e forse un po' lunghetto, anche se dal di dentro ogni partita sembra volare: 4 turni nella fase A e 4 nella fase B. Viste le poche fasdi diventa importante scegliere con fermezza una strategia e portarla fino in fondo.

RATING: 9
Lovely action-management, an intriguing auction mechanism, and quite possibly the most mis-read set of rules anywhere. The worst box inset known to man, but a very, very good and surprisingly lightweight game for all that.

The 2-player game has been much lauded, but with more players the focus switches from the auction to the Action Card distribution, and the game is all the richer for it. For the lengths to which it involves and engages its participants, this is deservedly Rudiger Dorn's masterwork.

Further Thoughts: I just can't find the flaws in this game any more, not since I junked the box insert! Upgraded to - and beyond - the elite.

Further, Further Thoughts: Ah...sometimes a quarter-century is enough. Downgraded from 10 to 8.5: this one doesn't quite grab me as often as it used to.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Goa feels like Puerto Rico thematically, but the mechanics and scoring are completely different. Goa is (slightly) more complex and quite long at 2hrs30 (4 player). Each player has their own separate board to track their holdings and there is a central 5x5 board of tiles which determines what can be auctioned.

The game centres around scoring points (mainly) by producing goods and putting them on ships in order to make yourself more efficient at the 5 main actions (producing goods, taxation, founding colonies, acquiring ships, drawing development cards). The cards make the game more random than PR’s plantation draw, but I don’t find it a problem.

There are 8 turns, each turn has 2 parts, the auction part and the main part where players take actions. Each player nominates a tile (or 2 if you are 1st player), the catch is that the tile you nominate must be adjacent to the tile nominated by the player before you. Then they are sold in a once round auction, so as auctioneer you get to hear everyone else’s bid then decide either to take their money or buy the tile yourself (paying your money to the bank). Also the 1st player marker is a purchasable commodity.

With money leaving the game and entering the game (through the taxation action, tiles and cards) the players collective decisions result in inflation or deflation of the game economy. This is significant since money circulates around via the auctions. This is distinct from the progressive devaluation of cash in PR as you get bonus points for most cash at the end, so you always want to have as much as you can get your hands on (but that’s also true of other game elements that need to be weighed up). Oh, money is also kept hidden, though I strongly prefer to house-rule it open.

The other main pressure acting against you besides money is the limited number of actions. You really want to do 5 or 6 things per turn but can only do about 3 or so. So you have to prioritise and decide which are your most important actions. Another agonizing dilemma is that you get more points if you get very specialised, but if you neglect to advance some of the tracks you'll run into problems which leads to some delicious decision points.

As a warning, the game is low on interaction outside of the auction, the theme is thinner than most German games, and it has a fairly hard learning curve because there are so many different tiles. As a new player I found it difficult to evaluate the tiles so I can see this leading to under/over bidding issues in a group of mixed experience. I expect it would still be less of a problem than a mixed PR group where one person is crafting too much. In other words it is less “new player friendly” than many games of similar complexity.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Rudiger Dorn does it again. One of the most pleasant experiences I've had gaming. I've played this two times and they've both been placid despite it being a victory-point Euro. Maybe it's building your own tiny colonial merchant empire away from other players or the beige colors of the components, but I felt very at peace (not bored) playing Goa. The auction mechanic works well here as there are several different tiles that have their unique value for any strategy. There are multiple paths to victory (although getting more actions per round seems to be the common denomination) and the set-up of the tiles changes all the time, forcing players to re-evaluate their strategies. While non-interaction with other players may be a downfall for other games in my opinion, I felt constantly engaged with my opponents during their turns. Enjoyable and always a fun game to play with friends who aren't afraid of "colonial"-themed games.

RATING: 8
Interesting auction phase followed by a solitaire-ish action phase.  The auction is what makes this game shine since players determine what tiles go up for bid and players must decide to sell their own tiles for profit or buy them instead.  The fact that if a player wins a tile through auction he pays THAT PLAYER instead of the bank is really what makes this auction great.  A player only pays the bank if he purchases his own tile.
 During the action phase, you are faced with many choices, most of which are good.  But you have to tailor your actions around the tiles you won in the auction phase.  Yeah, the game is a bit dry and not all the mechanics make thematic sense.  But I'm a euro guy so I obviously don't care.  I would never turn down a game of this.

UPDATE:  After a couple of dozen plays, I feel like I have played this one out.  It's still a euro that deserves classic status and I won't turn down a game of it when the situation arises, but I decided o put it up for trade.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Goa is one of my favorite games. The fantastic thing about Goa is the staggering number of choices you have, and the many ways they intertwine.  It favors having a strategy early on and nurturing it through the game. This is an uncommon trait in euros!

Is it multi player solitaire?  I think of all the euro-games this charge has been made to, Goa does deserve it to some degree, although not as much as Puerto Rico.  The action phase seems to be hardly interactive in any way, except that you do need to watch what the other players are up to, and try to figure out how much money they have (or play with open money for a very sharp game).  You need to spend your actions carefully to prepare yourself for the next auction.  But, observing other players' actions is not interaction.  

We play by letting players take all three regular actions together. It makes no real difference to the game (contrary to prevailing wisdom around here). After all players have played their regular actions, players may use any action cards to take extra actions.

The auctions, of course, are brutally interactive.  They get more and more cut-throat as the game proceeds.  Play this with two and just see how vicious it can be close to the end. Errors in optimization at any stage of the game will set you back, but errors in later rounds are naturally harder to recover from.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It may be all buttons and levers but I like the buttons and levers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a fantastic game.  I really like the spatial aspect of the auction, you have some control over what gets auctioned, but not complete control, and some knowledge of what will be auctioned in the future, but not complete knowledge.  Each turn presents a variety of tough decisions, and you can take your company in a number of different directions.  The game can be a little fragile; if one or more players runs out of money or doesn't value things properly, other players can benefit based on seating.  Additionally, the once-around auction demands that your valuing be right on the first try, because you don't get another chance.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 8
Rules are simple, but you need a lot of attention to get into them, provided the great number of pieces that the game has. The material is really gorgeous. The game is rather standard, not so similar to PR, rather a mix between PR and Siedler IMHO. You have to produce some resource, get it and use it to create greater productions, or money, or victory points, always in tention to decide how to use your new resources. Every moment there's a decision that can change your entire long term strategy. It also works very well with two players. One disappointment: there is no way to count the 3 actions played within a round, and this can create a little problem. Strange, because every single piece has its own place and every thing seems thought with a lot of accurancy. Maybe I just missed something.
=====
After some plays the score remains 8, although it feels more and more abstract, sometimes lonely, and very very very difficult. This is the point: the game is very brain-consuming, but it doesn't look to be very deep: you always have to manage a hundred of things, that's why it's difficult.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Another impressive economics game. Interesting, strategic, multiple ways of achieving your goals and a very intelligent auction mechanism  that really elevates this above the average.It's a true gamer's game that rewards planning, flexibility and creative playing. Luck also plays a role with the expedition cards and the colony foundation mechanism. Some people complained that its role is too important but in my (limited) experience it has never been an issue. Overall a great game and a very solid design. My favorite Rudiger Dorn game to date.UPDATE: I played again 4 player and I definitely prefer it with less players. It's not a bad game with 4, far from it, but there are other games that I prefer for that particular setup (PoF, PR, AoS, ...). Rating went down 9->8. Still an amazing 2p game... I think I would rate it a 9!

RATING: 8.5
-

A fantastic multi-faceted game with great replayability value and balance. It has some general similarities to The Princes of Florence, with lots of details to pay attention to. Fantastic player interaction and an incredible amount of tough decision making through out.

Goa is generally somewhat heavier and more involved than typical German style games.  The understanding of functions of the tiles and the Expedition cards require some careful attention to details, which may scare away first-timers, but after a couple of plays familiarity with the tiles and cards will enhance the understanding and enjoyment of the game.

-

[floatleft][ImageID=980805][/floatleft]
[b][u]Initial Impressions:[/u][/b]
Goa is a brilliant Euro game. It is one of the best game in it's genre. Goa almost feels like a pseudo-civilization game where players build up technology tracks to improve their civilizations. The auction mechanism is perfect. If I could rate this game an 11 I would. 

[floatright][ImageID=971814][/floatright][b][u]What I like:[/u][/b]
The auction. I really like the auction.

[b][u]What I dislike:[/u][/b]
Where the hell is an iPhone app already?!

[b][u]Overall:[/u][/b]
Of all the classic designer Eurogames I have played this is by far my favorite. 

Disclaimer: The latest edition of Goa seems to have changed some significant rules that I am not in favor of, specifically how the person auctioning can pay for their own auction at a -1 cost. I would rather play the original rule set. I am a Goa purest.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very enjoyable play of this classic has got me thinking about the word 'dry' when it comes to games, which is a derogation usually aimed at Euros (I'll leave Abstracts out of this). There is almost no theme here, which seems to be the qualification of 'dry', yet the game is undeniably good. Excuse me for an aside...

I can think of three other games I’ve played at my regular group that have been described as 'dry': Medina, Mombasa, and Kingsburg. I found the physical building of the buildings in Medina to be enough 'theme' for me. Mombasa is great, despite its wonky bookkeeping track, and as it's a re-themed 18XX (AFAIK), then the accusation is a fair cop. Kingsburg, though, was both dry and random which is what really made me never want to play it again.

So, Goa, I think, works despite being dry because the good mechanisms are bolstered by minimal randomness which is restricted to the settler draw. Spicy goodness.

RATING: 5
I had been looking forward to Goa for quite a while; it's a well-regarded Euro that sounded right up my alley. But I was rather disappointed by my first play. The meat of the game is optimisation, and players spent most of their time with eyes down on their own little 'spreadsheet', working out the best actions. The theme is almost non-existent -- why should ginger and cloves (or whatever) be just what you need to advance your ship-building technology? -- which makes the game feel very dry. The best part is the auction, but even then the once-around bidding reduces the feel of interaction. I can see that there are lots of subtleties that could be revealed over multiple plays (the inflation/deflation effects are interesting), but I'm not sure I have much desire to uncover them.

RATING: 8
Like the 2 phase approach - A limited action, resource optimization phase with a combined auction phase. Both mechanisms are well balanced. 
In the auctions you find yourself auctioning items that you know others want to get money to get what YOU want.
In the action phase you are trying to get the most of each action. To specialize gives you more VPs to be more balance gives you more actions which should help in getting a better position

The game plays well with 4, and I could see that it could scale down to lesser numbers and still play well.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
UPDATE: Traded this away. I really don't like auction games (except the blind variety). And I only enjoy it as a two-player game.

I love the action element of this game. It forces you to try to deduce what your opponents want. Although the mechanism to choose the elements to auction off is incredibly abstract, it adds some subtle interaction.

Unfortunately, the other part of the game -- the "develop your business" part is *dry*, time-consuming, and fiddly. You don't actually build anything; all you do is move gray cubes in one direction, one step at a time. Also, these steps, which improve your business, are too incremental; they don't jump in dramatic spurts like Puerto Rico, which adds to the dryness, and the "lack of dramatic arc" some have complained about. Indeed, this part of the game feels like a uneven conglomeration of every mechanic in which resources can be gathered and/or spent, complicating this game way too much, and adding way too much game play time (in the form of bookkeeping and squinting at your own and your opponent's player boards). To be fair, this conglomeration is tightly integrated with each resource dependent on another in a significantly different manner.

However, it is because of this conglomeration that makes the auction part great; the actions being auctioned off are so disparate and varied (and useful, unlike the special actions in Age of Steam) compared to other bidding games, which ultimately makes this element of the game constantly tense.

Basically, best heavy auction game, which loses (a lot) of points from undue (but perhaps necessary) complexity.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Outstanding bidding game, where each round the tiles to be auctioned are selected by players in a unique method where each selected tile must be adjacent to its predecessor. Actions are then taken to: improve one's strength in 5 resource tracks  (money, ships, goods, colonists, or expedition cards collected); to collect those resources; or to found colonies. There are many paths to victory, yet the game is so balanced that completely different approaches can result in a win by just one point. Very fun and interactive. Played with 2 and 3 (which was much better), yet to try with 4.

RATING: 7.82
My only play of this we played the auction rules incorrectly, and initially the game felt a little bit dry. However, on about the fourth round everybody's attempts do things collapsed. We realised that the economy was rather more complicated than we had thought, which is what makes it interesting. 
The game is played over eight rounds: in each round players bid for various tiles and then take three actions to develop their colony. These actions allow them to get better resources in future rounds and score victory points at the end of games. Fairly standard but the way they are put together makes the game challenging without creating a dominant strategy. 
Definitely one I'd like to try again.

RATING: 6
25-09-2015. Rated after 1 play with 3.
Goa is a fairly typical auction euro game.

You have a playerboard and a board where you can stock resources and 'discovered?' cities.  Each turn consists of auctioning several tiles (1 more than the number of players, I think) from a main board. You can get fields (resources), settlers and ships. You can use resources and ships to upgrade your playerboard. This is filled with special abilities you can improve.. like getting more money.

Game is quite ok. It's a fairly light euro-game, but it's really hard to see 'proper' paths. I finished my game last and gave away the win because I didn't know how to auction in round 1. It's difficult to.. 'judge' the value of a tile starting out, especially when you play gamers who played the game 100+ times.
That said, it was quite ok.

RATING: 9
This game sounds extremely complicated when someone first teaches it to you, but once the game starts going, everything clicks almost immediately, and the game seems surprisingly simple.  Much like Dorn's other games (Traders of Genoa, etc) there's a lot going on, but play proceeds very logically.  There are a lot of tough choices to be made, and never enough actions in your turn to do everything you want.  The different mechanics - from the auctions, to the plantaion production, to the action choices, to the technology advancement track, and all the different possible paths to victory mesh beautifully to create an intriguing and very original gameplay experience that despite it's uniqueness seems instantly familiar once you play it.  This game is a winner and belongs on every gamers shelf.  I'm going to get my own copy as soon as my game-buying budget re-fills.

RATING: 10
Record: 16-19
1st: 16
2nd: 13
3rd: 5
4th: 1

12/13/07: First game of Goa in a LONG time... I tied for first, lost out on tiebreakers! My own fault too, I screwed up the last 2 rounds something fierce!

04/20/07: Played my first 2p game today. I was blowing Tyler away, but he came back pretty big in the last round and we ended up tied - he won the tiebreaker (most money). Good comeback, Tyler!

01/11/07: I just got 2nd (to JOE) in a 4 player game because of tiebreakers! That's pretty frustrating, but it was a good game overall.

I pooh-pooh'ed this game for quite some time without even looking at it because I had remembered reading something on the web which made me think I wouldn't like it - something like it was formulaic, similar to Princes of Florence (which got old quick for me). A friend of mine got the game, opened it at my house and wanted to play it, but I couldn't make myself interested.

A few weeks ago I finally played the game, and much to my surprise it was ABSOLUTELY NOTHING like I thought it would be. And it was awesome.

My rating may go down over time, or when my 100% record gets broken, but for the time being I think this might be my favorite strategy game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
So this is where the multiplayer surplus-transfer auction began (2er incarnation seen in Lowenherz and Basari, multiplayer in Neue Heimat)! Choose things to put up for auction, and the winning bidder either pays you, or if you win, you pay the bank.

This is merged with some pseudo Genoa-like system to choose what gets auctioned (which I don't really see the spatial point of), and a tedious Splendor-like spreadsheet exercise to collect the necessary resource combinations to up your power tracks and increase efficiency. It's held together tenuously and arbitrarily by cold hard mechanisms.

Not enamored with my only play (2er), but I'd give it a try with 3 or 4 players, and try to focus on the auction instead of the uninspired rest of the game.

3+2

RATING: 10
Clever auction system; excellent game, but the cards are a little too powerful.

Goa has always been an excellent but flawed game; however, Z man has fixed it. Now it is a perfect game and I'm moving it up to a 10. The six development columns have been evened out in strength, so that there are more viable paths to victory and the auction system is still one of the best I've ever seen in a game. Each game is intense and full of tough choices.  If you don't have this version, you must go out and buy it immediately.

RATING: 8
Goa -- Played four-player.  I like games that impart the feeling that I'm building something -- from the map in Carcassonne to the simple societies in Settlers.  Somehow, this game gave me that feeling.  Both Maharaja and Goa integrate disparate game mechanics, but in Maharaja, I find myself stepping outside the game to notice the different mechanics, while in Goa I don't -- My immersion in the game is more complete.

Remember:  Play using the original Hans im Glück rules.  Namely, discard [i]before[/i] you draw (so that you only draw up to your hand limit), otherwise the exploration track is overpowered.

RATING: 6
A reasonable game, a cross between Princes of Florence and Cities and Knights of Catan. Suffers terribly from wild swings of luck that destroy hours of planning for no particular reason (theme, perhaps, but not an enjoyable reason, anyway).

Like PoF, you need to deal with what you get from the auction, and plan a game strategy around it. Like C&K, progression gives you more vps, better production and more bonus cards. If you like these games, you will probably like Goa.

After a very slight change (no flipping for colonies, just add 4 to your attempt), and no three player games (bad auctioning in three player), the game is somewhat more enjoyable, but still a weaker version of Princes of Florence, and dominated by whomever secures nutmeg early (in four player games). Two player seems best.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
First off, get someone to teach this to you. The rulebook is HORRIBLE. Dense and poorly written. The game however, is an above average yet typical style German game.

Players seek to manage their resources: money, found colonies, manage ships, harvest crops, etc. The bidding aspect seems to work well with more than 2 players.

The game is strangely broken into 2 phases: the first one is usually one where players build up their base resource productions and the second phase is where they crank up the VP scoring with missions.

A little too long for what it offers but like most solid German games, offers a plethora of options and limited actions to perform them all!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
We play with closed money, and do not keep track of it. As long as I have a reasonable idea of how much money is in the game, I feel I can make a reasonable bid. We are still figuring out strategies for progression, so I am thinking it will take a long time before we figure everything out.

As many others have pointed out before me, the auction mechanic works well even with two (although it does seem like it would be better with more). I like the spatial aspect of flag placement. 

A few more plays in, Michelle still doesn't understand how much to bid on anything. I don't think she's ever won? Of the new games this year, I think the only one she hates more is Agricola.

RATING: 8
A game that successfully blends an auction mechanic with resource management. So many decisions to make is this game: from the onset of the auction choices, to how you want to spend your resources to improve your ships, plantations, etc. It's like an unrestored hotrod, it could use some updating in the "eye-candy" department but underneath the hood there's a monster of a game engine. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Goa is a captivating game. It's the type of game that forces you to think 3 steps ahead and just when you think you've figured out a game plan, you're adapting a new strategy. 

There are two wonderful elements to Goa. The auction phase and the action phase. I would even venture to say the auction phase trumps that of Princes of Florence. This has quickly become one of my favorites.

The auction phase is critical to your gameplan. Try to bluff, try to win, but never let anyone know how much money you have in your hand.

RATING: 9
After 10-12 games of Goa, I have decided that the game is not really the second coming of PR and PoF.  It is a good game, to be sure, but I don't get the same "boy, is this game slick, or what?" feeling that I get from the other two games.<BR><BR>

As I noted before:<BR><BR>Goa has an excellent auction system, which allows for great flexibility and creativity in the auction choices, as well as the opportunity to purchase more than one item at auction.  Based up auction results, the player must then plot an expansion course which will maximize their dollars and resources won at auction.  Each player will be pursuing a different course, allowing for even more excellent decisions in future auction rounds.<BR><BR>One needs to realize, though, that the "Expedition Card Track" needs to be part of your gameplan in some fashion from the very beginning.  Pursuit of matching symbols cannot be ignored the whole game.<BR><BR>One distinct advantage that this game has over PR and PoF is the excellent 2 player game.  Even though a 2 player auction is usually quite dull, Goa's is very good, with extremely tough bidding decisions to be made.<BR><BR>I think that this one is an excellent game, but not PR.

RATING: 9
Zow, this game keeps me Engaged right to the end.  There are so many things going on in this game.  Tough decisions abound.  This is a perfect game for those that love the "make due with what you've got" mechanic.  I have a feeling this will always be one of my favorites.

The game isn't overly complex but there is just a ton of stuff to take in and work towards.  There are quite a few other game mechanics grafted into this one, but still feels quite original.  Certainly, one of the most innovative auction system. We play with the Roads and Boats rule where everyone plays their actions simultaniously unless someone calls for turn order.  If you think that this is a multi-player solitare game, prepare to be in last place.  Tragic that this is out of print already.

RATING: 8
First off, I've only played it 2p, albeit four times.  Second, it's a fantastic game, with that really solid sense of completeness like it's cut from whole cloth.  Which of course it isn't, but that's neither here nor there. This is a "by definition" BGG rating of 8 for me.  But I don't own it, and I'm not sure I ever will.  It takes a few plays before you really snuggle up to it, and I doubt my wife would have that kind of patience with a game like this (meaning the theme).

Personally, I love the look of the game, especially the surreal pilgrim and ship cards (Yeah, yeah.  They're Dutch.)  The spices are quaintly clunky, and the auction tiles are thick and pretty.  
Gameplay is rather subtle, especially the auction phase.  In the 2p game, you really need some idea (within a buck or two) of what your opponent has to spend.  Valuation of the flag is tricky, and it's easy to overspend on tiles.  The rest of the game, which is fiddling with advancing your income/capacity/exploration tracks, seems straightforward, but so far it's been pleasantly fraught with surprises.  One constant is that you can never have enough extra actions.  Much like Louis XIV, this game is usually won by the player who is still taking actions while everyone else watches.  Again, caveats all around, I've not played 3 or 4p.  

This is by far the dryest game I've played since I started with Euros.  But I think it's worth getting over that hump if you like meaty Euros.  Oh, yes, multi player solitaire, blah blah blah.

RATING: 8

RATING: 9
BestWith:2>4>3 [Auction/Action, Economic] 120 min. Note to self: If need to replace, make sure to get the older edition, not the 2006 reprint from China.
2011 Reprint changes:
* Pay 1 less (rather than 1 more) when buying your own auction item.
* Player 1 starts with 5 gold (rather than 7).

Tiles:
* Money tile now gives $3/turn instead of $4.
* Colonist track goes 2/4/5/6.
* Ship track goes 1/2/3/5/6.
* Spice requirements reversed for the "refill plantations" and "gain cash" tracks.
* Duty tile (A and B) is now 5 VP instead of 4.
* Two new tiles for each era, so 4 are removed for each era, but no more than 2 plantation tiles.
  These new tiles include:
  * Crop Rotation (A), a wild single plantation, but worth zero victory points.
  * Foreman (A), causing single plantation tiles to improve from one victory point each to three victory points each.
  * Favor (B), select a tile from the four A and four B tiles returned to the box during setup.
  * New Tile (B): "2 colonist, exchange colonists for ships/spices" (will allow spending those leftover colonists).
* Eight new tiles for the "Bonanza" variant/expansion.

Cards:
* "Advance with money" card is not an action modifier (and thus, effectively grants an extra action).
* Three '1' colonist cards are changed to '2' colonist (thus, the colony founding deck is more favorable).
* "Sell spice" card can sell a max of four spices
* A new symbol, the elephant, has been added. There are now 5 each of 6 symbols, instead of 6 each of 5 symbols.
* "Flip an extra colony card" card can be played after you see what your two colony cards are.

COMPONENTS (cosmetic):
* New tiles and chits to track the current round (A1/A2/A3/A4/B1/B2/B3/B4) and current action (1/2/3/extra)
* Stickers to put on the spice baggies, so that colorblind players can tell the difference between red nutmeg and green pepper
* Plantations and colonies now have the name of the plantation/colony printed on the tile (e.g., "Ginger," or "Quilon," or "Crop rotation")
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
I like the game. It's not very heavy, it's not a mind burner, but still has a couple of decisions to make. The only thing I don't like that much in the game is, along with the lack of interactivity, the fact that we can always make your own game. The resources never end, if you can't produce pepper that's ok, you can produce anything else because the result seems to be the same.
The balance on the development still seems to be the goal of the game and that's why I like it.

I tend to ignore the multiplayer solitaire aspect of this game, maybe because I love the theme and the art. I'm only expecting to play it again and again to see if I'll rate it higher.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[RGG 2004 (first printing) edition] {Colony, economic; development, auction, hand-management, utility cards; optimal 2 or 4p} A brain-teasing strategy game which is only suitable for veteran players because it's an unintuitive scoring game with small degree of player interaction. 

Rule is complicated and unintuitive but the game system is not complex. Goa is like a scoring game without a convincing theme. It has some luck elements (expedition column and the colony establishment); yet a proper strategy can overcome the luck elements. Goa is well-balanced (with the German rule) and no single strategy must win. Nevertheless, I usually start advancing the expedition column early to give myself more tactical alternatives. The limit of 24 actions requires very precise calculation each time; thus, Goa needs an extremely effective strategy to win and is unforgiving. I think additional actions are necessary to win. Goa is vulnerable to analysis paralysis. Don't play with slow players or perfectionists.

Goa has a unique auction mechanic, which makes Goa stand out from other strategy game. Actually, the auction is the cornerstone of Goa. The money flows from one player to another player in this system unless a player chooses to pay the bank and gets a tile himself. Therefore, a player can decide whether to get money / tile when placing his bid markers. In addition, the EV of the tile and money changes at each bid because of the closed economy, which makes it difficult to judge a reasonable price (I saw price ranging from 1 to 35 gold). 

In practice, Goa is like solitary games with 2 to 4 players playing together. Auction of tiles provide the only player interaction. After that, players can actually take actions themselves independently except for drawing of expedition cards and selecting colony. Therefore, don't expect loads of fun in Goa.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This is a classic that I've amazingly never managed to play, though it's been on my "Want to Play" radar for quite awhile. I was a little wary coming into it--after a bad brush with Princes of Florence, I sometimes treat classics with caution, for fear that I will have too high expectations. In this case, however, no such kid gloves were necessary. Goa was an incredibly entertaining game, albeit a schizophrenic one. There are several different mechanics all cobbled together, and yet, amazingly enough, it works. The auction placement mechanism adds a lot of interactivity and tension, the semi-closed auctions provide an interesting method for obtaining income, and the tech-leveling system provided several different methods of approaching the game. Goa is one of those games where you don't have nearly enough turns to do everything you want, and it works very well within that genre. I'm surprised and pleased with this classic, and am looking forward to getting another game of it in sometime.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Auction game centred around resource management. On each turn players have to bid on a number of tiles, each of which bestows a different benefit to the player - some provide resources, others extra ships or colonists, and some methods of scoring extra points etc. Once this is done actions are taken such as moving along one of five different tracks (ships, resource production, money, hand limit, colonists), activating one of the tracks to acquire the associated benefit, or attempting to colonise areas with your colonists. This is key to the game as the player who most efficiently progresses along the tracks will be able to perform more powerful actions on their turn in comparison to their opponents. Victory is determined by points awarded for the progress made along each track, the number of colonies established and any other for of scoring such as extra tiles or having the most money at the end of the game. 'Goa' provides an interesting challenge to the player and the game can take several paths determined by the tiles available at any one point. Victory points aren't particularly abundant either resulting in generally tight games.

RATING: 7
Only a couple of plays in the last 7 years. I really like this game too. What is wrong with me?




Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 1
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 3
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 1


Overall - 1

First Impression:

A decent auction/development game.

The mechanics of this game work pretty well and keep the players involved during the whole game. The development part of the game really rewards the players while keeping them racing forward, while the auction part doesn't allow the leaders race too much forward. The theme is really bad, not that well implemented and in the end lacking.

This appears to be a good euro, and scratches the same itch as Nations for me (although it lacks the theme and falls below in several other categories). However I find themeless games more and more hard to choose to play.

RATING: 9
In the same vein of Amun-Re and Princes of Florence.  The auction/action system is one that seems to produce winners for me.    This game does provide me with laughs, when people claim "bad" luck when failing to start a colony.... especially when they were counting on "good" luck to create it.  Bonus points for common misguided whines.  Anyways, time is your most valuable resource, and there are many ways to lose time.  Fun time, well balanced.  Just enough luck to keep things interesting.  It's good, its flawed, but its fun.  The big lightbulb moment is realizing everything needs to be reduced into number of actions, and set the auction cost/want to that.  It helps properly evaluate the auctions and compare seemingly different currencies, that only relate into number of actions.  Having a general guidline for price/action saved based on the current economy at the table, and you will fare well in this game.

RATING: 8
[thing=9216][/thing] is the sort of game of "old", when the games had "only" one major mechanic and one or two little ones to complement the other. A time of not several mini-games, but only a big one. And [thing=9216][/thing], though not literally big (it uses a reasonable amount of table, even with individual player boards).

A game round has two phases: in the first one, the first player chooses a place in the main board. From this spot the next player can choose one of the adjacent tiles to offer for bidding. The next player chooses a tile. And so forth until the first player chooses the last one. Then players bid on the tiles - like in [thing=12][/thing], everyone gives only one bid, no one has a chance do rise the amount given. The money is paid to the "owner" of the tile, and the player himself can buy his/hers own tile, if he/she pays more than the bid offered to the bank.

Next is the action phase: everyone has 3 actions, which can be: developing things (the ships, the production, the acquisition of money or colonist, etc) - for this is necessary to pay goods and ships; trying to colonize (Quilon, Cochin, Madras or Calicut); using one of the developments (getting ships, producing, getting money or cards - the colonist are passive, they give a value that helps in the colonization of those four places).

There are a few other things (like when all developments leave the level 0, the player get one action card; or the first one to reach the 6 or 10 points level in one of the rows, get a special card), but this is the basic of what goes on in the game. A few rounds of this and the game ends. The winner is the player with more points, which comes from:
- the level of development of the rows (goes from 0 to 10 points);
- the number of places colonized (goes from 1 to 10);
- the player with the most money in the end receives 3 points;
- special cards are worth 1 point (but they have symbols, so with two cards with the same symbol you will get 3 points; with 3, 6 points and with 4, 10 points);
- points from the tiles.

[thing=9216][/thing] is a great game, that requires though decisions, as with onlt three actions per round, something always will get pushed for the next round, or putted aside. Money wasn't really tight as I thought it would be (as with many other auction games) - usually everyone could by at least one tile, so the real questions are: which tile to buy? Wich to sell?

The interaction among players happen in the bidding phase. In the action phase is everyone for himself/herself. There is no limited action nor blocking someone of doing something. If the player wants to produce, it will happen. If the player want ships, he/she will get them. This means you must be aware of what the other players are doing when you choose the tile to offer and, when the bidding is happening, what the others will do with the tile they are getting. Because once they got it, there is nothing you can do.

Playing time is around 90 to 120 minutes.

I liked [thing=9216][/thing] a lot. It was probably the game in the Top 50 that I wanted to play the most and it delivered. A great game through and through.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

This is a very good example of how games used to be and are not like anymore. Simple rules, plenty of positive interaction and deep gameplay. It is a classic for a reason.

RATING: 8
I played Goa shortly after Princes of Florence and while they felt very similar in structure (both are a round of auctions followed by a round of actions building up your engine efficiently), I think I prefer Goa a bit more. Neither one is very interactive, but the auctions in Goa are more interesting and everyone is involved in every one. Furthermore, there is so much more to consider when building your engine in Goa and the choices are much less obvious, that I find the down time much less noticeable because I am so engrossed in my own situation.

Anyway, it is a rather quietly contemplative game, so I'll never love it, but it works for me far better than others in the genre that I've tried (PoF, Puerto Rico, Das Zepter)

RATING: 9
An incredibly well-done euro.  This is one of the pinnacles of the form.  Though the theme has been done to death and the mechanics initially seem dry, the gameplay is good enough that even the most vehement thematic gamers I know have been won over once they played this masterpiece.

Why is it so good?  There's no one thing that stands out about it, but it is balanced in so many attributes.  It's interactive but not chaotic, richly tactical but not strategically restrictive, and richly replayable but not difficult for newbies.

Games with semi-closed economies like this one can be fragile, but this has a very strong forward momentum and plenty of ways to make sure that nobody can break the system.  Timing is important, as infrastructure improvements are desperately needed early, but difficult to obtain.  That great feeling of too much to do, too few actions pervades through the entire game.  The goods intertwine in a very simple way, but have a dynamism that will force re-evaluation as the game goes on.

In an era of shelf churn, where most games get sold or traded after a few plays, this has stayed on my shelf for a long time, and will probably never leave.

RATING: 5
The endgame gets a bit drawn out and lengthy, but seasoned players can work through the end of their turn while the next player starts on his.  The various ways to get VPs make for a nice game, and the "multiplayer solitaire" issue means that this is a gentler sort of competition for crowds who prefer that sort of thing.  Like Amun-Re, though, there's definitely an issue with the random action cards giving a player a large advantage near the end of the game, but it's difficult to balance that against all the other options you have on your turn.  The last few times I've played the game, though, I've just become frustrated, and I find myself wishing that the game would end, three rounds before the end of the game.  That's probably not a good sign, but I still respect the design.

RATING: 7
I'm glad I stuck with this game past the first couple of plays. Before playing this game for the first time, read the rules carefully! On both initial plays we made mistakes that dragged out the game terribly and didn't give it a fair shake at all. After properly following the rules on how many turns to play (there really should be a component to help groups track this), the game is shorter, tenser, and more challenging -- that is: it turns out to be pretty much as advertised. Not much in the way of player interaction other than the auctions and trying to spend your resources better than others in order to get your share of "first awards" in the various development tracks. But a very good resource management game for those that like "build up from boostraps" games with little in the way of interaction (I have several regulars in my group that really prefer games like this to others with more direct player conflict).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played with 4 players and with 2. I like the 2 player games a little better than 4. 

It is essentially a resource management game where you build your spice trading empire. 

There are 2 parts to the game:
One is an auction phase where you fight for different resources that will help you to expand your business. The player interaction is all in this phase. Making others pay a little more for their items or denying them items is as important as grabbing something for yourself cheaply 

The second is an action phase in which you use the resources you gained in the auction phase. This phase is more solitaire and all about resource management and executing your broader plan, but you do want to keep an eye on what your opponents are doing.

All in all this is a great game with a lot different ways to get to your goal.
I do like it a bit more than Puerto Rico at the moment

RATING: 7
This is about as classic of a Euro as they come. When I first came to the geek, Goa was the game everyone referred to as the best auction game on the market. I'm not sure that claim still holds up today, but it's still a solid entry into any game night. Like most Euros, it looks more complicated than it is. The basic structure of the game is players auctioning resources that they then use to improve their technology board, which in turn lets them acquire even more actions and resources. It's quite simple once you get into the flow, on par with other classics of its day, such as El Grande and Tigris and Euphrates.

RATING: N/A
Competing Portuguese spice merchants colonizing India to export our goods back home. Common board setup of random 5x5 grid of upgrade tiles. Cards for ships, coin, exploring and colonist. Player plantation boards also track the 5 progress and scoring paths of ship building, spice harvest, tax, explore, and colonize.  Unique auction mechanics that limit what comes up for auction and the winner pays the player holding the auction.  A little dry and abstract for our group.

RATING: 7
I played it a couple of times with two and it didn't really tick.  However, it plays well with three and, one assumes, better still with four.

Complexity:  probably 4 out of 5; on a par with Puerto Rico, I'd say (lots of tiles to learn what they do, a decent amount of recall needed for the turn order, analysis can get quite deep).
Choice:  4 out of 5, quite squarely.  There's randomness in the initial set-up, and in whether you get to found a colony, but other than that, it's all based on the players' own choices.
Conversation:  I think 3-ish; the bidding round *is* the engine of the game, but after that, you're basically doing your own thing.

RATING: 7.25
I have played this game once now and it's a very interesting game. I've recently become more interested in games that have an economic element to them. These types of games are also lacking in my collection to date (along with auction game), so I thought that this was an opportunity to take a chance on something different.

While I enjoyed the engine building aspect of the the game, I realized how awful I am in auction games. It probably has more to do with my lack of exposure to those types of games and fully understanding what a good return is on my bid.

From an initial play of the game, I was pleasantly surprised. I'm definitely looking forward to trying it out again soon to see if my opinions on the game will change.

RATING: 7
Wow. Very impressed. A bit tricky to get the feel for just by the rules, but tons of options, lots of depth, virtually no down time and tough decisions make this a great game! Each individual action seems a bit fuzzy, though, as there are very few "bad" moves -- just shades of grey in how "good" they are at the time. [UPDATE] The more I've played, the more I'm convinced that the order of operations is largely irrelevant -- as long as you make progress, it's very hard to screw up. There are a few exceptions, but for the most part, the only thing you need to time is taxation to get a monetary advantage for bidding, or when to seize extra actions.

RATING: 8
This is a great Euro gamer's Euro game.  Dry, nearly themeless with strong rewards for good planning ahead.  The spatial auction works pretty well as on some turns folks feel like they have lots of choices and on other turns the 1st player can really control the flow of the auction.  However I found the real meat of the game to be in the ability growth and action taking of the game.  Add in a mix of special abilities vs set collection from the expedition cards and you have a lot of very interesting choices on your turns without really getting bogged down in AP... except perhaps on big spice harvest rounds as you plan out your turn based on spices in advance.

If you like "tech trees", interesting twists on auctions or dry Euros in general you should really give Goa a try. But for me it didn't have staying power as I'd rather play Hansa Teutonica for the better tech tree or Metropolys for the more interested spatial auction.

RATING: 6
The method utilized for the tile auction is a neat design.  However, I absolutely do not care for the bidding, even if it's just the somewhat painless once around.  Most of the time the value seem very arbitrary, it is pretty much at the whim of the other players.  There is almost no way you can determine the worth a given item besides how badly you want it.  Is the exploration track broken?  Probably not, at least I can't tell from just a single play.  Yet you cannot deny the advantage these cards confer to those who can and are drawing them at a faster rate than those who aren't.  The amount of free actions you can get, whether in ships, colonists, money or spices, simply cannot be overlooked.  My initial reaction to Goa is that it's an overhyped game undeserving of the high rating here.  I may try it again, but certainly would not miss it if I don't get the chance to.

RATING: 9
This is a brilliant abstract game in spices' clothing.  Trying to make progress in 6 categories and nab miscellaneous points over 8 turns with only 3 actions per turn is a blissful challenge.  Fortunately, some of the actions (and auctions) can grant you extra actions, and - needless to say - extra actions can carry you far in this game.  Player interaction is about as low as Princes of Florence, but like Princes of Florence and Puerto Rico, much of the fun is in trying to solve the puzzle of the current game as it unfolds, whilst your opponents do the same, competing for items you need for your grand plan.

RATING: 8.8
GOA新版修订了一些老版的规则，比如拍卖和探索等方面，另外增加了新的功能板块及可选规则，将游戏的平衡性更一步的提高。体验上有些类似“航海家”，玩家版上明确的将几种发展路线列举出来，玩家所要做的就是规划拍卖阶段的资金使用，然后利用自己发展路线上的优势面带动劣势面前进。游戏中的获得的额外行动奖励将带给玩家不错的优势。
【关键词】A/B阶段，拍卖放置规则，拍卖发起人，各类功能板块，3+N行动轮，平均/专属发展奖励，五类货物，四大殖民地

【推荐度】高【拍卖发展，中重度德式爱好者
